###begin article-title 0
###xml 14 16 14 16 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 31 34 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
Acid-evoked Ca2+ signalling in rat sensory neurones: effects of anoxia and aglycaemia
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 307 309 307 309 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 391 392 391 392 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 427 428 427 428 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 509 511 509 511 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 512 513 512 513 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 512 513 512 513 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 541 543 541 543 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 597 599 597 599 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 642 644 642 644 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 645 646 645 646 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 645 646 645 646 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 656 658 656 658 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 745 747 745 747 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 789 791 789 791 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 981 982 981 982 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E</italic>
###xml 989 990 989 990 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 1004 1005 1004 1005 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 1024 1025 1024 1025 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</italic>
###xml 1077 1078 1076 1077 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 1272 1274 1270 1272 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1305 1307 1303 1305 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1325 1327 1323 1325 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1542 1544 1540 1542 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1545 1546 1543 1544 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1545 1546 1543 1544 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1585 1587 1583 1585 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1633 1635 1631 1633 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1854 1856 1852 1854 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 346 349 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
Ischaemia excites sensory neurones (generating pain) and promotes calcitonin gene-related peptide release from nerve endings. Acidosis is thought to play a key role in mediating excitation via the activation of proton-sensitive cation channels. In this study, we investigated the effects of acidosis upon Ca2+ signalling in sensory neurones from rat dorsal root ganglia. Both hypercapnic (pHo 6.8) and metabolic-hypercapnic (pHo 6.2) acidosis caused a biphasic increase in cytosolic calcium concentration ([Ca2+]i). This comprised a brief Ca2+ transient (half-time approximately 30 s) caused by Ca2+ influx followed by a sustained rise in [Ca2+]i due to Ca2+ release from caffeine and cyclopiazonic acid-sensitive internal stores. Acid-evoked Ca2+ influx was unaffected by voltage-gated Ca2+-channel inhibition with nickel and acid sensing ion channel (ASIC) inhibition with amiloride but was blocked by inhibition of transient receptor potential vanilloid receptors (TRPV1) with (E)-3-(4-t-butylphenyl)-N-(2,3-dihydrobenzo[b][1,4] dioxin-6-yl)acrylamide (AMG 9810; 1 muM) and N-(4-tertiarybutylphenyl)-4-(3-cholorphyridin-2-yl) tetrahydropryazine-1(2H)-carbox-amide (BCTC; 1 muM). Combining acidosis with anoxia and aglycaemia increased the amplitude of both phases of Ca2+ elevation and prolonged the Ca2+ transient. The Ca2+ transient evoked by combined acidosis, aglycaemia and anoxia was also substantially blocked by AMG 9810 and BCTC and, to a lesser extent, by amiloride. In summary, the principle mechanisms mediating increase in [Ca2+]i in response to acidosis are a brief Ca2+ influx through TRPV1 followed by sustained Ca2+ release from internal stores. These effects are potentiated by anoxia and aglycaemia, conditions also prevalent in ischaemia. The effects of anoxia and aglycaemia are suggested to be largely due to the inhibition of Ca2+-clearance mechanisms and possible increase in the role of ASICs.
###end p 1

###begin title 4
Keywords
###end title 4
###begin title 5
Introduction
###end title 5
###begin p 6
###xml 396 397 396 397 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 482 484 458 460 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">20</xref>
###xml 486 488 462 464 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">21</xref>
###xml 490 492 466 468 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR59">59</xref>
###xml 494 497 470 473 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR109">109</xref>
###xml 558 559 534 535 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 711 712 687 688 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">5</xref>
###xml 714 716 690 692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR22">22</xref>
###xml 718 720 694 696 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR24">24</xref>
###xml 722 724 698 700 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR70">70</xref>
###xml 726 728 702 704 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR76">76</xref>
###xml 730 732 706 708 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR80">80</xref>
###xml 734 736 710 712 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR85">85</xref>
Tissue ischaemia leads to rapidly declining oxygen levels and diminished capacity for oxidative phosphorylation. The consequential switch to anaerobic metabolism, to maintain cellular ATP production, results in increased generation of metabolic acid. This, in combination with the lack of perfusion, culminates in a rapidly developing tissue acidosis. For example, during myocardial ischaemia, pHo falls to approximately6.8 within the first 8 min and approximately6.2 after 20 min [20, 21, 59, 109]. Tissue acidoses together with elevation of extracellular K+, ATP and bradykinin are all thought to play a role in the excitation of sensory neurones within ischaemic tissue and the generation of ischaemic pain [5, 22, 24, 70, 76, 80, 85].
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 87 89 87 89 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">12</xref>
###xml 91 93 91 93 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR68">68</xref>
###xml 354 356 354 356 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>
###xml 357 359 357 359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">48</xref>
###xml 361 363 361 363 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR99">99</xref>
###xml 475 477 475 477 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">46</xref>
###xml 478 480 478 480 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">48</xref>
###xml 482 485 482 485 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR100">100</xref>
###xml 593 595 593 595 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 597 599 597 599 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR78">78</xref>
###xml 725 727 725 727 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>
###xml 729 731 729 731 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR59">59</xref>
###xml 829 831 829 831 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">29</xref>
###xml 833 835 833 835 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR33">33</xref>
###xml 837 839 837 839 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR58">58</xref>
###xml 841 843 841 843 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR60">60</xref>
###xml 845 848 845 848 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR104">104</xref>
###xml 850 853 850 853 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR111">111</xref>
###xml 1088 1090 1088 1090 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">16</xref>
###xml 1092 1094 1092 1094 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">44</xref>
###xml 1096 1098 1096 1098 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR82">82</xref>
###xml 1156 1157 1156 1157 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 1164 1166 1164 1166 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1168 1169 1168 1169 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 1169 1171 1169 1171 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ca</sub>
###xml 1172 1173 1172 1173 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">P</italic>
###xml 1173 1175 1173 1175 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Na</sub>
###xml 1195 1196 1195 1196 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">8</xref>
###xml 1198 1200 1198 1200 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR32">32</xref>
###xml 1202 1204 1202 1204 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR96">96</xref>
###xml 1206 1208 1206 1208 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR97">97</xref>
Acid is thought to excite sensory neurones by activating pH-sensitive cation channels [12, 68]. Two main types of proton-gated cation channel have been found in sensory neurones: acid-sensing ion channels (ASICs) and the transient receptor potential vanilloid 1 receptor (TRPV1). ASICs are predominantly sodium selective and can be blocked by amiloride [46-48, 99]. The ASIC family contains four distinct genes, of which ASICs 1 and 3 are both expressed in sensory neurones [46-48, 100]. ASICs and, in particular, ASIC3 have been implicated in cardiac sensory neurone activation by ischaemia [10, 78]. Although thought to be primarily gated by protons, activation of ASIC3 may be further enhanced by the presence of lactate [41, 59]. TRPV1 is expressed predominantly in small sensory neurones from the dorsal root ganglia (DRG) [29, 33, 58, 60, 104, 111] and is a polymodal sensor. TRPV1 may be activated by capsaicin, heat, protons and proalgesic substances. This ability of TRPV1 to integrate both physical and chemical stimuli suggests that it plays a major role in pain transduction [16, 44, 82]. Unlike most ASICs, TRPV1 is highly permeable to both Na+ and Ca2+ (PCa/PNa between 1 and 10) [8, 32, 96, 97].
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 256 258 256 258 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR37">37</xref>
###xml 260 262 260 262 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR50">50</xref>
###xml 533 535 533 535 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 603 605 603 605 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 606 607 606 607 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 606 607 606 607 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 651 653 651 653 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 705 707 705 707 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 748 750 748 750 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 800 802 800 802 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 931 933 931 933 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1097 1098 1096 1097 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 1399 1401 1398 1400 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1463 1465 1462 1464 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1466 1467 1465 1466 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1466 1467 1465 1466 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1562 1564 1561 1563 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1577 1579 1576 1578 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 1717 1719 1716 1718 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1720 1721 1719 1720 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1720 1721 1719 1720 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
In addition to acting as pain sensors, sensory neurones also release neuropeptides during ischaemia (e.g. calcitonin gene-related peptide (CGRP) or substance P) which may act locally to modulate tissue function and/or blood flow within the ischaemic zone [37, 50]. Whilst electrical excitation of sensory nerves by acidosis/ischaemia most probably results directly from membrane depolarisation (due to activation of the above proton-gated cation channels), secretory responses are likely to be mediated via a rise in intracellular Ca2+. There are numerous factors that could contribute to changes in [Ca2+]i during ischaemia including voltage-gated Ca2+ entry secondary to electrical excitation, direct Ca2+ influx through proton-gated channels, Ca2+ release from internal stores and modulation of Ca2+ uptake, buffering or extrusion. In the present study, we have therefore investigated the effects of acidosis on intracellular Ca2+ regulation in small, capsaicin-sensitive, sensory neurones (15-30 mum) from cervicothoracic DRG. These neurones were exposed to four different acid stimuli with pHo values 6.8, 6.2 (with and without lactate), and 5.0 simulating the initial phase of an ischaemic event, more prolonged ischaemia and an extreme acidosis of the type typically used to study acid-sensitive cation channel function in vitro. Using a pharmacological approach, we have characterised the Ca2+-entry pathways and stores that contribute to elevation of [Ca2+]i during acidosis. In addition, since anoxia and aglycaemia can also have profound effects on Ca2+ metabolism [34], we combined these stimuli with acidosis to more closely simulate ischaemic conditions and to investigate their collective effect on [Ca2+]i.
###end p 8
###begin title 9
Materials and methods
###end title 9
###begin title 10
Neurone dissociation
###end title 10
###begin p 11
###xml 143 144 143 144 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 147 148 147 148 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">6</sub>
###xml 244 246 244 246 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 254 256 254 256 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 563 564 562 563 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 580 581 578 579 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 703 705 695 697 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 713 715 705 707 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 954 955 946 947 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">g</italic>
###xml 1310 1311 1299 1300 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">l</sc>
###xml 1455 1456 1438 1439 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 6 17 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">Wistar rats</span>
###xml 797 803 <span type="species:ncbi:9913">bovine</span>
Adult Wistar rats of either sex aged between 6 and 8 weeks (130-170 g) were sacrificed by an overdose of halothane (4%). Cervicothoracic DRG (C4-Th6) were removed under sterile conditions and were immediately transferred into cooled (on ice) Ca2+- and Mg2+-free phosphate-buffered saline (PBS), pH 7.4. After cleaning the ganglia from surrounding tissue, the ganglia were incubated in an enzymic dispersion media comprising 10 mg/ml collagenase type I (208 U/mg, Worthington, CLS-1, MON4393), 1 mg/ml trypsin (9.3 U/mg, Sigma, T-4665), in PBS and with 60 muM CaCl2 and 33 muM MgCl2. The ganglia were incubated at 37degreesC for 35 min. Following enzyme treatment, the ganglia were washed once in PBS (Ca2+- and Mg2+-free) and once in Dulbecco's modified Eagle's medium (DMEM; containing 10% fetal bovine serum, 1.2 mM l glutamine), before mechanical trituration in 1.5 ml of DMEM. The dissociated cells were then washed twice by centrifugation (at 1,000xg for 5 min) followed by resuspension in fresh DMEM. Following the final wash, the cell pellet was resuspended in 500 mul basal TNB-100 containing protein-lipid complex (Biochrom, Berlin, Germany), penicillin (100 IU/ml), streptomycin (100 mug/ml) and 10 muM/ml nerve growth factor (TNB). Following a second trituration, the neurones were seeded onto poly-l-lysine and laminin-coated coverslips (6 mm in diameter) and incubated in sterile culture dishes in a humidified chamber at 37degreesC and 5% CO2/95% air for 2 h. After this incubation period, a further 3-ml TNB was added to each culture dish. The neurones were then kept in the incubator for at least 30 min before being used for experiments. These neurones were typically used within 2 days of isolation.
###end p 11
###begin title 12
Fluorescence measurements
###end title 12
###begin p 13
###xml 931 932 921 922 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
Measurements of Fura-2 fluorescence were performed using a microspectrofluorometer based on an epifluorescence microscope (Nikon Diaphot 200, Japan) fitted with photomultiplier tubes (PMT; Thorn, EMI, UK) to detect emitted fluorescence and a motor driven monochromator (Cairn Instruments, Kent) with xenon lamp to provide the excitation light source. Fura-2 was excited alternately at 340 and 380 nm (+/-8 nm) for 250 ms at each wavelength with the cycle repeated at 1 Hz. Emitted fluorescence from Fura-2 was passed through a bandpass filter centre wavelength 510 nm (+/-20 nm). Bandpass filtered fluorescence was detected using a PMT air cooled to -20degreesC (Thorn, EMI, UK). The output from the PMT was integrated over each illumination period and recorded on a microcomputer using a micro CED1401 and Spike 4 software (Cambridge Electronic Design). For Fura-2, the ratio of fluorescence at 340 nm relative to that at 380 nm (R) was also calculated and recorded using Spike 4 software.
###end p 13
###begin title 14
Selection and superfusion of neurones
###end title 14
###begin p 15
Neurones were placed in a recording chamber with a volume of approximately 100 mul mounted on the stage of the epifluorescence microscope (see below). This chamber was perfused with solutions at a flow rate of approximately 2 ml min. Solutions were delivered from reservoirs kept in a water bath to the recording chamber via medical grade stainless steel tubing articulated by short sections of Pharmed tubing (Norton performance plastics, UK). A mechanically driven two-way tap which allowed a rapid change between two different solutions was placed within a few inches of the recording chamber. A heating coil was placed around a short section of tubing between the tap and the chamber to ensure solutions remained at 37degreesC. This arrangement allows rapid solution exchange and tight control over the gas content and temperature of solutions.
###end p 15
###begin p 16
###xml 227 229 226 228 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 230 231 229 230 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 230 231 229 230 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
Neurones were observed and fluorescence recorded through a X40 fluorescence grade objective (n.a. 1.30). Sensory neurones were initially selected by soma size (15-30 mum) and then tested for a response (a robust increase in [Ca2+]i) to capsaicin. This capsaicin test was usually conducted at the end of the experiment and proved positive in >80% of the neurones selected by the above size criteria. Only capsaicin-positive neurones are included in this study.
###end p 16
###begin title 17
Loading of neurones with Fura-2-AM
###end title 17
###begin p 18
###xml 435 436 434 435 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 444 445 443 444 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
To introduce Fura-2 into neurones, they were incubated in either a 4-(2-hydroxyethyl)-1-piperazineethanesulfonic acid (HEPES) buffered saline (for in vivo calibrations) or a bicarbonate buffered saline (for experiments) containing 5 muM Fura-2-AM (Molecular Probes, Leiden, The Netherlands) at room temperature for 25 min in a dark chamber. The HEPES buffered saline used comprised (in millimolars): HEPES 20, glucose 11, KCl 4.5, MgCl2 1, CaCl2 2.5, and NaCl 117, pH 7.4 at room temperature.
###end p 18
###begin title 19
In vivo calibration of Fura-2
###end title 19
###begin p 20
###xml 61 62 61 62 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 70 72 70 72 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 442 443 441 442 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 451 453 450 452 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 750 751 742 743 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 751 754 743 746 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">min</sub>
###xml 756 758 748 750 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">30</xref>
###xml 803 804 795 796 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 812 814 804 806 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 872 873 864 865 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1164 1165 1155 1156 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1165 1168 1156 1159 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 1211 1213 1202 1204 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1261 1263 1252 1254 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1272 1273 1263 1264 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1273 1275 1264 1266 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f2</sub>
###xml 1276 1277 1267 1268 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1277 1279 1268 1270 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b2</sub>
###xml 1348 1349 1339 1340 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1349 1352 1340 1343 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">min</sub>
###xml 1354 1355 1345 1346 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1355 1358 1346 1349 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 1364 1365 1355 1356 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1365 1367 1356 1358 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f2</sub>
###xml 1368 1369 1359 1360 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1369 1371 1360 1362 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b2</sub>
###xml 1488 1490 1479 1481 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1495 1496 1486 1487 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1499 1500 1490 1491 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1500 1503 1491 1494 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">min</sub>
###xml 1506 1507 1497 1498 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1507 1510 1498 1501 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 1513 1514 1504 1505 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 1518 1519 1509 1510 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1519 1521 1510 1512 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f2</sub>
###xml 1522 1523 1513 1514 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S</italic>
###xml 1523 1525 1514 1516 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b2</sub>
###xml 1532 1534 1523 1525 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">30</xref>
Fura-2-loaded neurones (see above) were incubated in a high-K+ zero-Ca2+ HEPES buffered solution (consisting of 150 mM KCl, 5 mM NaCl, 1 mM ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid and 1 mM ethylene glycol tetraacetic acid (EGTA)) containing 10 muM ionomycin (Sigma, Dorset, UK) for 10-20 min. After this incubation, the neurones were placed in a flow through incubation chamber mounted on the microspectrofluorometer and perfused with the same high-K+ zero-Ca2+ HEPES solution but containing 1 muM ionomycin and at 37degreesC. After a further 5-min incubation in this solution, Fura-2 fluorescence was recorded from five identified sensory neurones. The ratio of fluorescence obtained under these conditions was deemed equivalent to the calibration constant Rmin [30]. The perfusate was then changed to a high-K+ high-Ca2+ HEPES saline containing 150 mM KCl, 5 mM NaCl, 10 mM CaCl2 and 1 muM ionomycin. The change in fluorescence ratio was followed in one of the five identified neurones until it reached a new stable value, and then the fluorescence ratio in it and the other four identified neurones were recorded and deemed to be equivalent to the calibration constant Rmax. The ratio of fluorescence at 380 nm in Ca2+-free buffer divided by that obtained in high Ca2+ buffer (Sf2/Sb2) was also calculated for each neurone. The mean values obtained for Rmin, Rmax and (Sf2/Sb2) were then used to calibrate measurements of the fluorescence ratio in subsequent experiments using the equation [Ca2+] = (R - Rmin)/(Rmax - R) x Sf2/Sb2 x Kd [30].
###end p 20
###begin title 21
Solutions
###end title 21
###begin p 22
###xml 101 102 101 102 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 112 113 112 113 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 120 121 120 121 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">3</sub>
###xml 121 122 121 122 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 231 233 231 233 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 257 258 257 258 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 489 490 489 490 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 595 596 589 590 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 635 636 629 630 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 778 779 772 773 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3</sub>
###xml 829 830 823 824 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 854 855 848 849 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 909 910 903 904 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3</sub>
###xml 983 984 977 978 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1090 1091 1084 1085 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1101 1102 1095 1096 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1207 1208 1195 1196 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
The standard bicarbonate buffered Tyrode solutions contained (in millimolars) NaCl 117, KCl 4.5, CaCl2 2.5, MgCl2 1, HCO3- 23 and glucose 11. Glucose-free Tyrode solution was prepared by replacing glucose with 11 mM sucrose. For Ca2+-free solution, the CaCl2 was omitted and 1 mM EGTA was added. For Tyrode solution with elevated KCl concentration (50 mM), the NaCl concentration was reduced to 71.5 mM; all other constituents remained the same. Equilibration of these solutions with 5% CO2 and 95% air achieved normocapnic conditions with pH 7.4 at 37degreesC. Moderate hypercapnic acidosis (pHo 6.8) was achieved by increasing the CO2 content of the equilibrating gas from 5% to 20%. To achieve a combined respiratory and metabolic acidosis, Tyrode solution with reduced NaHCO3 (10 mM, 130 mM NaCl) was equilibrated with 20% CO2, which resulted in a pHo 6.2. pH 5.0 solutions were attained by lowering NaHCO3 to 2 mM (and increasing NaCl to 138 mM) whilst equilibrating with 20% CO2. Solutions simulating a lactic acidosis contained (in millimolars) Na-lactate 15, NaCl 126, KCl 4.5, CaCl2 2.5, MgCl2 1 and glucose 11. The pH of this solution was adjusted to 6.2 at 37degreesC by equilibration with 20% CO2 and addition of NaOH.
###end p 22
###begin p 23
###xml 142 143 142 143 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">x</italic>
###xml 147 148 147 148 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 157 158 157 158 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">x</italic>
###xml 161 162 161 162 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 206 207 206 207 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 208 209 208 209 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 210 211 210 211 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 213 215 213 215 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR72">72</xref>
###xml 321 322 321 322 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 323 324 323 324 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 325 326 325 326 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
Anoxic solutions were generated by replacing the air in the gas used to equilibrate the above solutions with nitrogen (i.e. gas mixtures were x% CO2 + 100 - x% N2) and with the further addition of 0.5 mM Na2S2O4 [72] following 15-30 min prior equilibration with the appropriate oxygen-free gas mixture. The addition of Na2S2O4 did not cause any detectable change in solution pH. All solutions were equilibrated with appropriate gas mixes at 37degreesC in a water bath for at least 30 min before use.
###end p 23
###begin title 24
Drugs
###end title 24
###begin p 25
###xml 1 2 1 2 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">E</italic>
###xml 9 10 9 10 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 24 25 24 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 44 45 44 45 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</italic>
###xml 94 95 93 94 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 277 278 273 274 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 485 486 479 480 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 501 502 495 496 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 518 519 511 512 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 535 536 528 529 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3</sub>
###xml 619 620 612 613 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
(E)-3-(4-t-butylphenyl)-N-(2,3-dihydrobenzo[b][1,4] dioxin-6-yl)acrylamide (AMG 9810, 1 muM), N-(4-tertiarybutylphenyl)-4-(3-cholorphyridin-2-yl) tetrahydropryazine-1(2H)-carbox-amide (BCTC, 1 muM), capsazepine (CPZ; 10 muM), cyclopiazonic acid (CPA; 10 muM), carbonyl cyanide-p-trifluoromethoxyphenylhydrazone (FCCP; 1 muM) and amiloride (100 muM) containing solutions were prepared from stock solutions in dimethyl sulfoxide. Capsaicin was added from stock solutions in ethanol. NiCl2 (2.5 mM), CdCl2 (100 muM), MnCl2 (1 mM) and GdCl3 (0.5-1 mM) containing solutions were prepared from 0.5 to 1 M stock solutions in H2O.
###end p 25
###begin title 26
Statistics
###end title 26
###begin p 27
###xml 127 128 125 126 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 468 469 466 467 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
Values were expressed as mean +/- standard error of mean (SEM). Statistical significance was tested using the paired Student's t test, or Wilcoxon signed-rank test for experiments with non-Gaussian distribution. Statistical testing of in vitro calibration data was performed using one-way analysis of variance and post hoc analyses were carried out using Bonferroni's multiple comparison, calculated by SPSS 12.0 software for windows. Level of significance was set at p < 0.05.
###end p 27
###begin title 28
Results
###end title 28
###begin title 29
###xml 27 29 27 29 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 30 31 30 31 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 30 31 30 31 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
Acidosis evoked rise in [Ca2+]i in sensory neurones
###end title 29
###begin p 30
###xml 137 139 137 139 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 140 141 140 141 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 140 141 140 141 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 219 220 219 220 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 242 243 242 243 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">3</sub>
###xml 243 244 243 244 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 248 249 248 249 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 303 304 303 304 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 317 318 317 318 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">3</sub>
###xml 318 319 318 319 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 330 331 330 331 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 360 361 360 361 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 472 473 472 473 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 485 486 485 486 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">3</sub>
###xml 486 487 486 487 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 491 492 491 492 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
Tissue acidosis is believed to be a major activator of sensory neurones transmitting ischaemic pain. Here, we investigated changes in [Ca2+]i in response to acidosis. Four different types of acidosis were tested: 20% CO2 in normal, 23 mM, HCO3- (pHo 6.8) simulating a simple hypercapnic acidosis; 20% CO2 in 10 mM HCO3- and 20% CO2 in 15 mM Na-lactate (both pHo 6.2) simulating a mixed hypercapnic and metabolic acidosis (with and without lactate ions present); and 20% CO2 in 2 mM HCO3- (pHo 5.0) simulating a very severe hypercapnic/metabolic acidosis.
###end p 30
###begin p 31
###xml 73 74 73 74 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 112 114 112 114 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 115 116 115 116 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 115 116 115 116 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 134 136 134 136 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 137 138 137 138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 137 138 137 138 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 190 192 190 192 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 244 246 244 246 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 247 248 247 248 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 247 248 247 248 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 302 304 302 304 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a</xref>
###xml 353 355 353 355 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>

###xml 389 390 389 390 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 389 390 389 390 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 466 468 466 468 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1c</xref>
###xml 526 528 526 528 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 585 587 585 587 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 588 589 588 589 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 588 589 588 589 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 668 670 668 670 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1b</xref>
###xml 673 679 673 679 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;1</label>
###xml 720 722 720 722 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 723 724 723 724 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 723 724 723 724 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 746 747 746 747 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 748 749 748 749 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">c</bold>
###xml 782 783 782 783 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 787 788 787 788 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 822 823 822 823 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 827 828 827 828 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 840 842 840 842 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 843 844 843 844 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 843 844 843 844 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 924 926 924 926 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 963 965 963 965 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1039 1041 1039 1041 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>

###xml 1042 1043 1042 1043 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1078 1079 1078 1079 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 1084 1085 1084 1085 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">c</bold>
###xml 1112 1114 1112 1114 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1177 1179 1177 1179 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1180 1181 1180 1181 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1180 1181 1180 1181 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1223 1224 1223 1224 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">c</bold>
###xml 1302 1304 1302 1304 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1305 1306 1305 1306 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1305 1306 1305 1306 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1357 1358 1357 1358 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 1373 1375 1369 1371 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1376 1377 1372 1373 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1376 1377 1372 1373 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1500 1501 1492 1493 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 1504 1505 1496 1497 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</bold>
###xml 1547 1549 1539 1541 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1582 1583 1574 1575 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 1642 1644 1634 1636 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1648 1649 1640 1641 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 1659 1660 1651 1652 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e</bold>
###xml 1662 1663 1654 1655 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f</bold>
###xml 1680 1682 1672 1674 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1725 1726 1717 1718 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1730 1731 1722 1723 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 1785 1786 1777 1778 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f</bold>
###xml 1824 1826 1816 1818 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1953 1954 1945 1946 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 1955 1956 1947 1948 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">c</bold>
###xml 1961 1962 1953 1954 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e</bold>
###xml 2004 2019 1996 2011 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">horizontal bars</italic>
###xml 2021 2031 2013 2023 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Bar charts</italic>
###xml 2089 2100 2081 2092 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">parenthesis</italic>
###xml 679 2100 679 2092 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="32">Extracellular acidosis evokes rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in sensory neurones. <bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold> Effects of a hypercapnic (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8) and a mixed acidosis (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) on [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in capsaicin-sensitive neurones in the presence and absence of extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup>. In the presence of extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup>, acidosis evoked an initial transient followed by a sustained rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in the majority of neurones (e.g. <bold>a</bold> and <bold>c</bold>). The amplitude of this Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transient amplitude was defined as the maximal increase in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> within the first minute of exposure (see <bold>c</bold>). The amplitude of the sustained rise was defined as the mean increase in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> during the final 30&#160;s of the exposure period (see <bold>a</bold>). The &#916;[Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> of the transients were thus defined as the difference between the sustained rise and the transient peak (&#916;trans., see <bold>a</bold>). <bold>d</bold> Comparison of the amplitude of initial Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by acidosis pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8 or 6.2 in the presence and absence of extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> (**<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01). <bold>e</bold>, <bold>f</bold> Comparison of Ca<sup>2+</sup> responses evoked by mixed acidosis (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, pH<sub>o</sub>&#8201;6.2) in the presence and absence of lactate (15&#160;mM). <bold>f</bold> Comparison of the amplitude of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> transient in response to lactic acidosis normalised to the response to acidosis in the absence of lactate. Time scale bars in <bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold> and <bold>e</bold> 200&#160;s. Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Bar charts</italic> present means + SEM; numbers of experiments are given in <italic>parenthesis</italic></p>

###xml 2100 2100 2092 2092 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig1_HTML" id="MO1"/>
###xml 673 2100 673 2092 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig1"><label>Fig.&#160;1</label><caption><p textid="32">Extracellular acidosis evokes rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in sensory neurones. <bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold> Effects of a hypercapnic (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8) and a mixed acidosis (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) on [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in capsaicin-sensitive neurones in the presence and absence of extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup>. In the majority of sensory neurones (65%) exposure to acidosis (either pHo 6.8, 6.2 or 5.0) evoked a rise in [Ca2+]i. This rise in [Ca2+]i typically had a biphasic kinetic with an initial Ca2+ transient leading into a sustained elevation of [Ca2+]i which remained throughout the exposure to acid (Fig. 1a). In a few neurones, however, only an initial Ca2+ transient was obvious with [Ca2+]i returning back to baseline whilst still under acidic conditions (e.g. Fig. 1c). Another small subgroup of neurones lacked an obvious Ca2+ transient and instead responded with a rapid rise in [Ca2+]i up to a plateau that was then sustained throughout exposure to acidosis (Fig. 1b). In the presence of extracellular Ca2+, acidosis evoked an initial transient followed by a sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in the majority of neurones (e.g. a and c). The amplitude of this Ca2+-transient amplitude was defined as the maximal increase in [Ca2+]i within the first minute of exposure (see c). The amplitude of the sustained rise was defined as the mean increase in [Ca2+]i during the final 30 s of the exposure period (see a). The Delta[Ca2+]i of the transients were thus defined as the difference between the sustained rise and the transient peak (Deltatrans., see a). d Comparison of the amplitude of initial Ca2+ transients evoked by acidosis pHo 6.8 or 6.2 in the presence and absence of extracellular Ca2+ (**p < 0.01). e, f Comparison of Ca2+ responses evoked by mixed acidosis (20% CO2, pHo 6.2) in the presence and absence of lactate (15 mM). f Comparison of the amplitude of the Ca2+ transient in response to lactic acidosis normalised to the response to acidosis in the absence of lactate. Time scale bars in a-c and e 200 s. Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Bar charts present means + SEM; numbers of experiments are given in parenthesis

###begin p 33
###xml 38 40 38 40 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 98 100 98 100 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 101 102 101 102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 101 102 101 102 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 192 194 192 194 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1c</xref>
###xml 260 262 260 262 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 263 264 263 264 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 263 264 263 264 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 333 335 333 335 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a</xref>
###xml 350 352 346 348 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 353 354 349 350 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 353 354 349 350 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 489 491 481 483 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a</xref>
###xml 498 500 490 492 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 567 569 555 557 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 570 571 558 559 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 570 571 558 559 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 676 678 664 666 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 679 680 667 668 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 679 680 667 668 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 704 706 692 694 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 774 776 762 764 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 777 778 765 766 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 777 778 765 766 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 814 815 802 803 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 815 818 803 806 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">1/2</sub>
The peak amplitudes of acid-induced Ca2+ transients were quantified as the maximal increase in [Ca2+]i attained within the first minute of exposure to acidosis relative to baseline (e.g. Fig. 1c). The sustained elevation was defined as the averaged rise in [Ca2+]i from the final 30 s of acidosis exposure relative to baseline (Fig. 1a). The Delta[Ca2+]i of the transients (Delta trans) were thus defined as the difference between the sustained elevation and the transient peak (e.g. Fig. 1a). A Ca2+ transient was only considered to have occurred where this Delta[Ca2+]i measurement was greater than the mean + 2 times the standard deviation of the sustained elevation in [Ca2+]i. The duration of the Ca2+ transient was characterised by measuring the time taken for the [Ca2+]i to fall to half of the peak value (t1/2).
###end p 33
###begin p 34
###xml 41 43 41 43 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 115 116 111 112 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 143 144 137 138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 215 216 209 210 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 242 243 234 235 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 270 272 262 264 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 319 320 311 312 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">o</sub>
###xml 320 322 312 314 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">2+</sup>
###xml 349 350 339 340 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 359 360 349 350 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 415 416 403 404 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 423 424 411 412 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 489 497 477 485 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1a, c, d</xref>
###xml 568 570 556 558 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 571 572 559 560 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 571 572 559 560 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 657 659 645 647 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 699 701 687 689 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 702 703 690 691 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 702 703 690 691 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 724 726 712 714 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 769 771 757 759 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 816 817 804 805 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 817 820 805 808 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">1/2</sub>
###xml 843 844 829 830 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 850 851 836 837 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 882 883 866 867 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 889 890 873 874 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
In neurones responding with an initial Ca2+ transient, the mean Deltatrans evoked by hypercapnic acidosis alone (pHo 6.8) was 112 +/- 24.5 nM (n = 10) and that evoked by a combined hypercapnic/metabolic acidosis (pHo 6.2) was 871 +/- 352 nM (n = 7). These acid-evoked Ca2+ transients were substantially attenuated in Cao2+-free media to 7 +/- 0 nM (n = 10, **p < 0.01) for a hypercapnic acidosis and 120 +/- 22 nM (n = 7, *p < 0.05) for a combined hypercapnic/metabolic acidosis (see Fig. 1a, c, d). Thus, for both types of acid stimuli, the transient elevation in [Ca2+]i in response to acidosis was strongly dependent upon the presence of extracellular Ca2+. In contrast, the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i was unaffected by Ca2+-free solutions (see below). Acid-evoked Ca2+ transients were often relatively brief with t1/2 = 27.8 +/- 1.1 s at pHo 6.8 (n = 27) and 28.1 +/- 1.3 s at pHo 6.2 (n = 27).
###end p 34
###begin p 35
###xml 194 196 194 196 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>
###xml 252 253 252 253 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 336 337 336 337 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 392 394 392 394 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 516 518 516 518 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 555 556 553 554 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 563 564 561 562 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 595 596 593 594 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 612 613 610 611 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 620 622 618 620 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1f</xref>
Acidosis due to tissue ischaemia is often accompanied by an increase in lactate from anaerobic respiration. Lactate has been reported to augment acid-evoked inward currents in sensory neurones [41]. We therefore compared a simple mixed acidosis (20% CO2, pH 6.2) with an equivalent acidosis in the presence of 15 mM lactate (also 20% CO2, pH 6.2). Eighty percent of neurones showed similar Ca2+ transients to both stimuli, and in these neurones, there was no significant difference between the amplitudes of these Ca2+ transients (Na-lactate 106 +/- 11%, n = 26, p = 0.442, data normalised to pHo 6.2 with 20% CO2; Fig. 1f). A few neurones responded only to one or other of the two stimuli.
###end p 35
###begin title 36
###xml 53 55 53 55 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Correlation between capsaicin- and acidosis-evoked Ca2+ transients
###end title 36
###begin p 37
###xml 214 216 214 216 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 292 294 292 294 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 295 296 295 296 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 295 296 295 296 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 439 441 439 441 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 502 503 502 503 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 509 510 509 510 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">r</italic>
###xml 519 520 519 520 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 529 530 529 530 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 543 544 543 544 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 550 551 550 551 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">r</italic>
###xml 560 561 560 561 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>

###xml 2243 2243 2239 2239 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig2_HTML" id="MO2"/>
###xml 1370 2243 1366 2239 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig2"><label>Fig.&#160;2</label><caption><p textid="38">Correlation between response to capsaicin and acidosis. <bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold> Correlation between maximum amplitudes of Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by acidosis vs. capsaicin (100&#160;nM). In this study, we have focussed upon sensory neurones selected by morphology and sensitivity to capsaicin. The capsaicin receptor (TRPV1) is also acid sensitive and would be expected to contribute to acid-evoked Ca2+ influx described above. We therefore sought to compare the amplitude of [Ca2+]i responses to capsaicin (100 nM) with those evoked by acidosis. A significant positive correlation was observed between the amplitude of the Ca2+ transient evoked by capsaicin (100 nM) and that evoked by pHo 6.8 (r = 0.49, p < 0.05, n = 22) and pHo 5.0 (r = 0.69, p < 0.05, n = 11; Fig. 2a, c), but not by pHo 6.2 (r = 0.4, p = 0.081, n = 20; Fig. 2b). It is, however, evident that this correlation was not particularly strong. We also noted a striking paradox in that a significant number of neurones (approximately 15-20%) responding to capsaicin did not respond to acidosis with a transient Ca2+ influx. This failure to respond to acidosis could not be attributed to low levels of functional expression of the capsaicin receptor since we found no significant difference between the amplitude of the capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients in acid responders (308 +/- 78 nM, n = 39) vs. acid nonresponders (295 +/- 86 nM, n = 12, p = 0.886; Fig. 2d). The importance of TRPV1 and other Ca2+ influx pathways in mediating acid-evoked Ca2+ influx was therefore investigated further. Fig. 2Correlation between response to capsaicin and acidosis. a-c Correlation between maximum amplitudes of Ca2+ transients evoked by acidosis vs. capsaicin (100 nM). There was a weak positive correlation between the Ca2+ response to capsaicin and that to pHo 6.8 and 5.0 (r = 0.49, p < 0.05, n = 22 for pHo 6.8; r = 0.69, p < 0.05, n = 11 for pHo 5.0) but not pHo 6.2 (r = 0.4, p = 0.081, n = 20). d The maximum amplitudes of [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin (100 nM) in neurones responding (responder) or not responding (nonresponder) to external acidosis with a biphasic rise in [Ca2+]i. There was no difference in the [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin in these two subgroups (p = 0.886). e The amplitudes of Ca2+ transients evoked by extracellular acidosis significantly increase with falling pHo values (1 compared to pHo 6.8; 2 compared to pHo 6.2; **p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001)
###end p 37

###begin title 39
###xml 49 51 49 51 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Influence of divalent and trivalent cations on Ca2+-entry pathways
###end title 39
###begin p 40
###xml 80 82 80 82 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 83 84 83 84 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 83 84 83 84 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 99 101 99 101 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 144 146 144 146 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 200 202 200 202 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 203 204 203 204 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 203 204 203 204 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 249 251 249 251 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
The former experiments indicated that external acidosis has a dual action on [Ca2+]i. An initial Ca2+ transient was evoked by activation of a Ca2+-entry pathway, whereas the sustained elevation of [Ca2+]i was triggered independently from external Ca2+.
###end p 40

###xml 2482 2482 2475 2475 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig3_HTML" id="MO3"/>
###xml 1380 2482 1376 2475 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig3"><label>Fig.&#160;3</label><caption><p textid="42">Inhibition of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-entry pathways by divalent and trivalent cations. <bold>a</bold> Voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> entry triggered by KCl exposure (50&#160;mM for 5&#160;s) is almost fully inhibited by 100&#160;&#956;M Cd<sup>2+</sup> (to 11% of control). <bold>b</bold> Application of Cd<sup>2+</sup> (100&#160;&#956;M) in Ca<sup>2+</sup>-containing solution amplified the [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> response to capsaicin (100&#160;nM). Note also that the [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> response to capsaicin in the presence of Cd<sup>2+</sup> often failed to fully recover. <bold>c</bold> In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solution, capsazepine (10&#160;&#956;M) prevented a rise in Fura-2 fluorescence in response to capsaicin and Cd<sup>2+</sup> application (note that following second exposure to capsaicin and Cd<sup>2+</sup> in Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free medium lacking capsazepine Fura-2 fluorescence increased dramatically and did not recover). <bold>d</bold> Inhibition of voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx (50&#160;mM KCl) by 2.5&#160;mM Ni<sup>2+</sup>. <bold>e</bold> Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) enhanced Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by capsaicin. <bold>f</bold> Gd<sup>3+</sup> (0.5&#160;mM) inhibited almost completely capsaicin evoked rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub>. The response to capsaicin recovered partially during a final capsaicin application omitting Gd<sup>3+</sup>. Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic>, 100&#160;s</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig3_HTML" id="MO3"/></fig>
In order to determine the Ca2+-entry pathways involved in mediating the transient response to acidosis, we needed to find ways of selectively inhibiting the different types of Ca2+-permeable channels that may be involved. Divalent and trivalent cations are often useful in this respect. Cd2+ is widely used to block voltage-gated Ca2+ channels especially in electrophysiological experiments. In these sensory neurones, Cd2+ (100 muM) similarly inhibited voltage-gated Ca2+ entry elicited by 50 mM KCl (by 89% +/- 7%, n = 5; Fig. 3a). The application of capsaicin in the presence of Cd2+ (100 muM), however, led to a rise in fluorescence ratio that exceeded that observed in the absence of Cd2+ and either did not recover or recovered only partially within the observation period (Fig. 3b). Removal of external Ca2+ failed to inhibit the effect of capsaicin in the presence of Cd2+ although it was inhibited by capsazepine (Fig. 3c). These data suggest that Cd2+ is able to permeate TRPV1 channels. Once inside the cell, Cd2+ will inevitably bind to Fura-2, as this has a much higher affinity for Cd2+ than for Ca2+, which will cause similar changes in Fura-2 fluorescence to that observed with Ca2+ [36]. Thus, Cd2+ is inappropriate for use as a blocker of voltage-gated Ca2+ entry under conditions in which TRPV1 channels might be active (e.g. such as in responses to acidosis). Fig. 3Inhibition of Ca2+-entry pathways by divalent and trivalent cations. a Voltage-gated Ca2+ entry triggered by KCl exposure (50 mM for 5 s) is almost fully inhibited by 100 muM Cd2+ (to 11% of control). b Application of Cd2+ (100 muM) in Ca2+-containing solution amplified the [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin (100 nM). Note also that the [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin in the presence of Cd2+ often failed to fully recover. c In Ca2+-free solution, capsazepine (10 muM) prevented a rise in Fura-2 fluorescence in response to capsaicin and Cd2+ application (note that following second exposure to capsaicin and Cd2+ in Ca2+-free medium lacking capsazepine Fura-2 fluorescence increased dramatically and did not recover). d Inhibition of voltage-gated Ca2+ influx (50 mM KCl) by 2.5 mM Ni2+. e Ni2+ (2.5 mM) enhanced Ca2+ transients evoked by capsaicin. f Gd3+ (0.5 mM) inhibited almost completely capsaicin evoked rise in [Ca2+]i. The response to capsaicin recovered partially during a final capsaicin application omitting Gd3+. Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars, 100 s
###end p 41

###xml 1101 2449 1099 2447 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="44">Mn<sup>2+</sup> quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. This <italic>figure</italic> shows Fura-2 fluorescence at each individual excitation wavelength (340 and 380&#160;nm) and the excitation ratio (340:380). In <bold>a</bold>, voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx is triggered by brief depolarisation using KCl (50&#160;mM for 5&#160;s) causing a transient rise in Fura-2 fluorescence ratio. In the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, depolarisation by KCl in the presence of 1&#160;mM Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) does not cause quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. Thus, Ni<sup>2+</sup> blocks Mn<sup>2+</sup> entry through voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels. In <bold>b</bold>, application of capsaicin also evokes a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solutions and in the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous application of capsaicin and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) resulted in a rapid quench of Fura-2 fluorescence at both wavelengths. Thus, activation of capsaicin receptors causes Mn<sup>2+</sup> influx independently of voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup>-channel activity. <bold>c</bold> Exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub>&#8201;6.2) triggered a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) caused a immediate quench of Fura-2 fluorescence of both individual wavelengths. Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic>, 100&#160;s</p>
###xml 1101 2449 1099 2447 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="44">Mn<sup>2+</sup> quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solutions and in the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous application of capsaicin and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) resulted in a rapid quench of Fura-2 fluorescence at both wavelengths. Thus, activation of capsaicin receptors causes Mn<sup>2+</sup> influx independently of voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup>-channel activity. <bold>c</bold> Exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub>&#8201;6.2) triggered a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) caused a immediate quench of Fura-2 fluorescence of both individual wavelengths. Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic>, 100&#160;s</p></caption>
###xml 2449 2449 2447 2447 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig4_HTML" id="MO4"/>
###xml 1095 2449 1093 2447 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig4"><label>Fig.&#160;4</label><caption><p textid="44">Mn<sup>2+</sup> quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. This <italic>figure</italic> shows Fura-2 fluorescence at each individual excitation wavelength (340 and 380&#160;nm) and the excitation ratio (340:380). In <bold>a</bold>, voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx is triggered by brief depolarisation using KCl (50&#160;mM for 5&#160;s) causing a transient rise in Fura-2 fluorescence ratio. In the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, depolarisation by KCl in the presence of 1&#160;mM Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) does not cause quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. Thus, Ni<sup>2+</sup> blocks Mn<sup>2+</sup> entry through voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels. In <bold>b</bold>, application of capsaicin also evokes a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solutions and in the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous application of capsaicin and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) resulted in a rapid quench of Fura-2 fluorescence at both wavelengths. Thus, activation of capsaicin receptors causes Mn<sup>2+</sup> influx independently of voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup>-channel activity. <bold>c</bold> Exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub>&#8201;6.2) triggered a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) to block voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels, the simultaneous exposure to acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (1&#160;mM) caused a immediate quench of Fura-2 fluorescence of both individual wavelengths. Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic>, 100&#160;s</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig4_HTML" id="MO4"/></fig>
Ni2+ at relatively high (millimolars) concentrations can also be used to block voltage-gated Ca2+ entry [62]; 2.5 mM Ni2+ thus blocked high-K+ (voltage-gated) Ca2+ influx (Fig. 3d) in these neurones but it did not inhibit the response to capsaicin; instead in the presence of Ni2+, we measured a slightly enhanced rise in [Ca2+]i (130 +/- 10%, n = 5, compared to control; Fig. 3e). This enhanced response to capsaicin in the presence of Ni2+ recovered back to baseline after wash out of capsaicin (unlike that seen in the presence of Cd2+). Mn2+ ions can also pass through many Ca2+ permeable channels and, upon gaining access to the cytosol, will quench Fura-2 fluorescence. Fura-2 fluorescence quenching by Mn2+ is therefore a useful technique with which to monitor the activation of various Ca2+-entry pathways. As shown in Fig. 4a, 2.5 mM Ni2+ prevents high K+-induced Fura-2 quenching by Mn2+ but does not prevent capsaicin induced Fura-2 quenching by Mn2+ (Fig. 4b). Ni2+ is therefore a good blocker of voltage-gated Ca2+ entry but has little effect upon Ca2+ or Mn2+ entry through TRPV1. Fig. 4Mn2+ quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. This figure shows Fura-2 fluorescence at each individual excitation wavelength (340 and 380 nm) and the excitation ratio (340:380). In a, voltage-gated Ca2+ influx is triggered by brief depolarisation using KCl (50 mM for 5 s) causing a transient rise in Fura-2 fluorescence ratio. In the presence of Ni2+ (2.5 mM) to block voltage-gated Ca2+ channels, depolarisation by KCl in the presence of 1 mM Mn2+ (1 mM) does not cause quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence. Thus, Ni2+ blocks Mn2+ entry through voltage-gated Ca2+ channels. In b, application of capsaicin also evokes a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In Ca2+-free solutions and in the presence of Ni2+ (2.5 mM) to block voltage-gated Ca2+ channels, the simultaneous application of capsaicin and Mn2+ (1 mM) resulted in a rapid quench of Fura-2 fluorescence at both wavelengths. Thus, activation of capsaicin receptors causes Mn2+ influx independently of voltage-gated Ca2+-channel activity. c Exposure to acidosis (pHo 6.2) triggered a transient rise of Fura-2 ratio. In the presence of Ni2+ (2.5 mM) to block voltage-gated Ca2+ channels, the simultaneous exposure to acidosis (pHo 6.2) and Mn2+ (1 mM) caused a immediate quench of Fura-2 fluorescence of both individual wavelengths. Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars, 100 s
###end p 43

###begin p 45
###xml 53 55 53 55 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 67 69 67 69 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 102 104 102 104 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 145 147 145 147 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 148 149 148 149 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 148 149 148 149 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 194 195 192 193 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 201 202 199 200 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 367 368 363 364 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 374 375 370 371 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 410 412 406 408 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3f</xref>
###xml 417 419 413 415 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 476 478 472 474 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 552 554 548 550 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 565 567 561 563 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR62">62</xref>
###xml 600 601 596 597 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="fn" rid="Fn1">1</xref>
We next tested the effects of the trivalent cation Gd3+ upon the Ca2+ response evoked by capsaicin. Gd3+ (0.5 mM) substantially inhibited the [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin (reduced to 6 +/- 4%, n = 6, p < 0.001 compared to control). This inhibition appeared to be only partially reversible (the third exposure to capsaicin following wash of gadolinium was 23 +/- 7%, n = 6, p < 0.01, compared to control; Fig. 3f). Gd3+ is therefore an effective inhibitor of TRPV1-mediated Ca2+ entry although it lacks specificity, i.e. it also blocks voltage-gated Ca2+ channels [62]. Reversibility may also be slow1.
###end p 45
###begin p 46
###xml 14 16 14 16 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 40 42 40 42 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 49 51 49 51 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 69 71 69 71 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 77 79 77 79 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 150 152 150 152 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 179 181 179 181 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 205 207 205 207 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 377 379 377 379 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
In summary, Ni2+ blocks voltage-gated Ca2+ and Mn2+ entry, but not Ca2+ or Mn2+ influx through TRPV1. Gadolinium (0.5 mM) blocks both voltage-gated Ca2+ channels and TRPV1, and Cd2+ blocks voltage-gated Ca2+ entry but actually permeates TRPV1 sufficiently well to induce a major increase in Fura-2 fluorescence. These features can therefore be used to discriminate between a Ca2+-influx pathway utilising voltage-gated channels and one using TRPV1.
###end p 46
###begin title 47
###xml 21 23 21 23 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Acidosis-activated Ca2+-entry pathways in DRG neurones
###end title 47
###begin p 48
###xml 37 39 37 39 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 166 168 166 168 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 194 195 194 195 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
In order to determine the route of Ca2+ entry in response to acidosis, we have studied the effects of a variety of pharmacological agents upon the amplitude of the Ca2+ transient generated at pHo 6.8, 6.2 and 5.0. In these experiments, control acid stimuli were applied before and after exposure to the pharmacological agent and the test acid stimulus; data were then normalised to the amplitude of the first control response (set as 100%).
###end p 48

A control exposure to acidosis was followed by an exposure to acidosis in the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (2.5&#160;mM) and finally by a second exposure to acidosis omitting Ni<sup>2+</sup>. <bold>a</bold> Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8 were unaffected by Ni<sup>2+</sup>. <bold>b</bold> Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2 were enhanced in the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and the post-Ni<sup>2+</sup> (control) response to pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2 was also augmented when compared to the first control response. <bold>c</bold> Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients in response to pH<sub>o</sub> 5.0 were unaffected by Ni<sup>2+</sup> although the post-Ni<sup>2+</sup> control response was again enhanced compared to the first control. <bold>d</bold> Summary of the effects of Ni<sup>2+</sup> on Ca<sup>2+</sup> transient response to acidosis. The data are presented as mean + SEM. Number for each experiment (<italic>n</italic>&#8201;=&#8201;6). Data on the effects of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solutions upon acid-evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients are included for comparison. Statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (*<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.05; **<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01; ***<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.001). Exposure periods in <bold>a</bold>&#8211;c are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic> in all recordings, 200&#160;s</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig5_HTML" id="MO5"/></fig>
Ni2+ failed to inhibit the Ca2+ transients evoked in response to pHo 6.8, 6.2 or 5.0 (Fig. 5). Indeed, there was some evidence that at pHo 6.2, Ni2+ enhanced the response to acidosis (276 +/- 42%, n = 5, p < 0.01) when compared to the first control. We also noted a tendency for the second, post-Ni2+, control response (recovery) to be amplified relative to the first control (Fig. 5d) at pHo 6.8 (127 +/- 10%, n = 6, p < 0.05), 6.2 (230 +/- 87%, p < 0.05, n = 5) and 5.0 (248 +/- 62%, n = 5, p < 0.05). Acidosis (pH 6.2) also activated Mn2+ influx and Fura-2 fluorescence quenching in the presence of 2.5 mM Ni2+ (Fig. 4c). Fig. 5Effects of Ni2+ on acid-evoked Ca2+ transients. Three different levels of external acidosis were used (pHo 6.8, a; 6.2, b; 5.0, c). A control exposure to acidosis was followed by an exposure to acidosis in the presence of Ni2+ (2.5 mM) and finally by a second exposure to acidosis omitting Ni2+. a Ca2+ transients evoked by pHo 6.8 were unaffected by Ni2+. b Ca2+ transients evoked by pHo 6.2 were enhanced in the presence of Ni2+ and the post-Ni2+ (control) response to pHo 6.2 was also augmented when compared to the first control response. c Ca2+ transients in response to pHo 5.0 were unaffected by Ni2+ although the post-Ni2+ control response was again enhanced compared to the first control. d Summary of the effects of Ni2+ on Ca2+ transient response to acidosis. The data are presented as mean + SEM. Number for each experiment (n = 6). Data on the effects of Ca2+-free solutions upon acid-evoked Ca2+ transients are included for comparison. Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (*p < 0.05; **p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001). Exposure periods in a-c are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars in all recordings, 200 s
###end p 49
