Gadolinium (Gd3+; 0.5 mM) completely blocked Ca2+ transients evoked by pHo 6.8 in all neurones investigated (n = 5) and almost completely blocked the response to pHo 6.2 (only one neurone in five showed any response; Fig. 6). Gadolinium also suppressed the second control (recovery) response to pHo 6.8 but not that to pHo 6.2. Application of capsaicin (100 nM) at the end of each experiment and after a rest period of several minutes evoked robust Ca2+ transients confirming that any effects of Gd3+ upon TRPV1 channels were ultimately reversible (Fig. 6). Fig. 6Effects of Gd3+ and Cd2+ on acidosis-evoked Ca2+ transients. a Gd3+ (0.5 mM) completely inhibited Ca2+ transients as a response to pHo 6.8. Ca2+ transients did not recover during a final exposure to pHo 6.8; however, the final application of capsaicin (100 nM), to characterise the neurones, still evoked a rise in [Ca2+]i. b Ca2+ transients evoked by pHo 6.2 were significantly reduced by Gd3+ (0.5 mM) below 10% compared to control transients. Following Gd3+ removal, the Ca2+ response to pHo 6.2 recovered fully. c In the presence of 100 muM Cd2+, acidosis evoked a strong increase of Fura-2 fluorescence which only partially recovered. d Summary of the effects of Gd3+ on Ca2+ transients evoked by acidosis (normalised to the first control transient, arbitrarily set as 100%). Values are presented as mean + SEM (n = 6 for each condition). Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (**p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001). Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars in all recordings, 200 s

###begin p 53
###xml 49 51 49 51 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 331 333 331 333 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6c</xref>
###xml 431 433 431 433 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Application of acid stimuli in the presence of Cd2+ caused a massive rise in Fura-2 fluorescence that almost reached saturation (i.e. the maximal value for the fluorescence ratio encountered during Fura-2 calibration). Following removal of acidosis, the fluorescence ratio did not fully recover during the observation period (Fig. 6c). This observation is similar to findings seen during capsaicin application in the presence of Cd2+.
###end p 53

###xml 2326 3288 2313 3273 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="58">Effects of BCTC and AMG 9810 (AMG) on acid (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients. <bold>a</bold>, <bold>d</bold> BCTC (1&#160;&#956;M) reduced Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by a mixed acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2, 20% CO<sub>2</sub>) to 22%. Breaks in recordings (<bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold>) before the second control acidic stimulus were approximately 10&#160;min (to try to facilitate reversal of effects of BCTC and AMG 9810). <italic>Time scale bars</italic> in all recordings, 200&#160;s. Statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (**<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01; ***<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.001, number of experiments are in <italic>parenthesis</italic>). <italic>rec.</italic> recovery, i.e. second postdrug control acid stimulus; lactate&#8201;=&#8201;15&#160;mM Na-lactate</p>
###xml 2326 3288 2313 3273 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="58">Effects of BCTC and AMG 9810 (AMG) on acid (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients. <bold>a</bold>, <bold>d</bold> BCTC (1&#160;&#956;M) reduced Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by a mixed acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2, 20% CO<sub>2</sub>) to 22%. The effects of BCTC were only partially reversible. <bold>b</bold>, <bold>d</bold> AMG 9810 (AMG, 1&#160;&#956;M) reduced mixed acidosis-evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients to 15% when compared to first control acidic stimulus. The effects of AMG were also only partially reversible. <bold>c</bold>, <bold>d</bold> BCTC and AMG 9810 significantly inhibited Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by a lactic acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2, 20% CO<sub>2</sub> 15&#160;mM Na-lactate). Breaks in recordings (<bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold>) before the second control acidic stimulus were approximately 10&#160;min (to try to facilitate reversal of effects of BCTC and AMG 9810). <italic>Time scale bars</italic> in all recordings, 200&#160;s. Statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (**<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01; ***<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.001, number of experiments are in <italic>parenthesis</italic>). <italic>rec.</italic> recovery, i.e. second postdrug control acid stimulus; lactate&#8201;=&#8201;15&#160;mM Na-lactate</p></caption>
###xml 3288 3288 3273 3273 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig9_HTML" id="MO9"/>
###xml 2320 3288 2307 3273 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig9"><label>Fig.&#160;9</label><caption><p textid="58">Effects of BCTC and AMG 9810 (AMG) on acid (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2) evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients. <bold>a</bold>, <bold>d</bold> BCTC (1&#160;&#956;M) reduced Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by a mixed acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2, 20% CO<sub>2</sub>) to 22%. The effects of BCTC were only partially reversible. <bold>b</bold>, <bold>d</bold> AMG 9810 (AMG, 1&#160;&#956;M) reduced mixed acidosis-evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients to 15% when compared to first control acidic stimulus. The effects of AMG were also only partially reversible. <bold>c</bold>, <bold>d</bold> BCTC and AMG 9810 significantly inhibited Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by a lactic acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2, 20% CO<sub>2</sub> 15&#160;mM Na-lactate). Breaks in recordings (<bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold>) before the second control acidic stimulus were approximately 10&#160;min (to try to facilitate reversal of effects of BCTC and AMG 9810). <italic>Time scale bars</italic> in all recordings, 200&#160;s. Statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (**<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01; ***<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.001, number of experiments are in <italic>parenthesis</italic>). <italic>rec.</italic> recovery, i.e. second postdrug control acid stimulus; lactate&#8201;=&#8201;15&#160;mM Na-lactate</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig9_HTML" id="MO9"/></fig>
CPZ (10 muM), an antagonist of capsaicin-mediated activation of TRPV1 [81], strongly inhibited the Ca2+ response to capsaicin (0.1-1 muM) as expected (p < 0.01, n = 6; see Supplementary material 1). In contrast, the Ca2+ transients evoked by three different acid stimuli were not attenuated by CPZ. Instead, there was a tendency for the Ca2+ transients in response to acidosis to be amplified slightly by the application of CPZ; this was significant at pHo 6.2 (205 +/- 60%, n = 6, p < 0.05 when compared to first control). Ca2+ transients evoked by the final, control, exposure to pHo 6.2 or 5.0 were also significantly larger than the first control Ca2+ transients (Fig. 8). Acid-evoked (pHo 6.2) Ca2+ transients were greatly reduced by BCTC (1 muM) to 22 +/- 5% (n = 12, p < 0.01). AMG 9810 also substantially reduced initial Ca2+ transients to 15 +/- 3% (n = 18, p < 0.001; Fig. 9b, d) when compared to first control. Ca2+ transients evoked in response to lactic acidosis (15 mM Na-lactate, pHo 6.2) were similarly reduced by both BCTC (to 15 +/- 4%, n = 17, p < 0.001) and by AMG 9810 (to 11 +/- 3%, n = 8, p < 0.01; Fig. 9c, d). For both BCTC and AMG 9810, the Ca2+-transient response to acidosis remained reduced compared to initial controls after washout of the drug (Fig. 9d). A similar lack of rapid/full reversibility was also noted in the ability of both BCTC and AMG 9810 to antagonise responses to capsaicin (see Supplementary material 1). Fig. 8a-c Effects of CPZ on acidosis-evoked Ca2+ transients. Experiments were conducted using three different levels of acidosis pH 6.8 (a), pH 6.2 (b) and pH 5.0 (c). Note that in contrast to its effects on capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients, capsazepine failed to inhibit acid-evoked Ca2+ transients. d Mean data from experiments in a-c. Ca2+ transients were normalised to control Ca2+ transients (set to 100%). Note that instead of inhibiting the response to acidosis, CPZ amplified the response to pHo 6.2. The effects of removal of extracellular Ca2+ on the acid-evoked Ca2+ response are included for comparison. Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Number of each experimental condition: n = 6. Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (*p < 0.05; **p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001). Time scale bars in all other recordings, 200 sFig. 9Effects of BCTC and AMG 9810 (AMG) on acid (pHo 6.2) evoked Ca2+ transients. a, d BCTC (1 muM) reduced Ca2+ transients evoked by a mixed acidosis (pHo 6.2, 20% CO2) to 22%. The effects of BCTC were only partially reversible. b, d AMG 9810 (AMG, 1 muM) reduced mixed acidosis-evoked Ca2+ transients to 15% when compared to first control acidic stimulus. The effects of AMG were also only partially reversible. c, d BCTC and AMG 9810 significantly inhibited Ca2+ transients evoked by a lactic acidosis (pHo 6.2, 20% CO2 15 mM Na-lactate). Breaks in recordings (a-c) before the second control acidic stimulus were approximately 10 min (to try to facilitate reversal of effects of BCTC and AMG 9810). Time scale bars in all recordings, 200 s. Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (**p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001, number of experiments are in parenthesis). rec. recovery, i.e. second postdrug control acid stimulus; lactate = 15 mM Na-lactate

###begin title 59
###xml 32 34 32 34 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Acidosis triggers a sustained Ca2+-release from internal stores
###end title 59
###begin p 60
###xml 25 27 25 27 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 28 29 28 29 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 28 29 28 29 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 63 64 63 64 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 86 87 84 85 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 132 133 130 131 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 156 157 152 153 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 248 250 244 246 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 251 252 247 248 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 251 252 247 248 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 287 288 283 284 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 324 326 320 322 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 327 328 323 324 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 327 328 323 324 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 350 352 346 348 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 371 372 367 368 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 391 392 385 386 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 399 400 393 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 416 417 410 411 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 436 437 428 429 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 443 444 435 436 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
The sustained rise in [Ca2+]i evoked by hypercapnic acidosis pHo 6.8 was 52 +/- 6 nM (n = 17) and that evoked by a mixed acidosis pHo 6.2 was 73 +/- 13 nM (n = 13). There was no statistically significant difference between the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in response to these two stimuli (p = 0.263). The sustained rise in [Ca2+]i was not reduced by Ca2+-free solutions (pHo 6.8, 42 +/- 5 nM (n = 10, p = 0.223) and pHo 6.2, 38 +/- 7 nM (n = 7, p = 0.097)).
###end p 60

###xml 931 1636 923 1626 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig10"><label>Fig.&#160;10</label><caption><p textid="62">ER Ca<sup>2+</sup> stores contribute to the acidosis evoked sustained rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub>. <bold>a</bold>&#8211;<bold>c</bold> In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free media acidosis (pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8) evoked a sustained rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub>. <bold>a</bold> Effects of ER store depletion with CPA (10&#160;&#956;M) on the acid evoked rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub>. <bold>b</bold> Effects of ER store depletion with caffeine (30&#160;mM). <bold>c</bold> Effects of mitochondrial Ca<sup>2+</sup>-store depletion with the uncoupler FCCP (1&#160;&#956;M). <bold>d</bold> Summary of effects of CPA, caffeine and FCCP on the acidosis evoked rise in [Ca<sup>2+</sup>]<sub><italic>i</italic></sub> in Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free media. Statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (**<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01; *<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.05 compared to acidic exposure in Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free solution). Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic>, 200&#160;s</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig10_HTML" id="MO10"/></fig>
In further experiments, we investigated whether this sustained Cao2+-independent rise in [Ca2+]i was due to acid-induced Ca2+ liberation from intracellular Ca2+ stores. Since we observed no differences between the sustained rises in [Ca2+]i evoked either by pHo 6.8 or pHo 6.2, the following series of experiments were conducted using a single acid stimulus (hypercapnic acidosis pHo 6.8). Depletion of endoplasmic reticulum (ER) stores using CPA (10 muM, a sarco-/endoplasmic reticulum Ca2+ ATPase (SERCA) inhibitor) significantly reduced the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i to 24 +/- 12 nM (n = 4, p < 0.05; Fig. 10a, d). Application of caffeine (30 mM) to deplete ER stores also reduced the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i to 11 +/- 2.4 nM (n = 5, p < 0.01; Fig. 10b, d). Depletion of mitochondrial Ca2+ stores by FCCP (1 muM) did not influence the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i evoked by pHo 6.8 (55 +/- 11 nM, n = 6, p = 0.235; Fig. 10c, d). Fig. 10ER Ca2+ stores contribute to the acidosis evoked sustained rise in [Ca2+]i. a-c In Ca2+-free media acidosis (pHo 6.8) evoked a sustained rise in [Ca2+]i. a Effects of ER store depletion with CPA (10 muM) on the acid evoked rise in [Ca2+]i. b Effects of ER store depletion with caffeine (30 mM). c Effects of mitochondrial Ca2+-store depletion with the uncoupler FCCP (1 muM). d Summary of effects of CPA, caffeine and FCCP on the acidosis evoked rise in [Ca2+]i in Ca2+-free media. Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (**p < 0.01; *p < 0.05 compared to acidic exposure in Ca2+-free solution). Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars, 200 s
###end p 61

###begin title 63
###xml 28 30 28 30 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 31 32 31 32 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 31 32 31 32 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
Effects of anoxia on the [Ca2+]i response to acidosis
###end title 63
###begin p 64
In vivo, the intense acidosis that accompanies ischaemia is primarily a consequence of the anaerobic generation of lactic acid due to a lack of oxygen delivery. Ischaemic acidosis is consequently invariably accompanied by anoxia. The following experiments were therefore conducted to investigate the effects of oxygen deprivation on the neuronal response to acidosis.
###end p 64

Exposure to anoxia plus hypercapnic acidosis (pHo 6.8) evoked Ca2+ transients with a peak Delta[Ca2+]i of 353 +/- 214 nM (n = 8; Fig. 11b). These Ca2+ transients were significantly larger (p < 0.05, n = 8) than those evoked by pHo 6.8 alone (112 +/- 60 nM). Anoxia plus a mixed acidosis (pHo 6.2) also evoked Ca2+ transients (498 +/- 25 nM, n = 7) that were significantly (p < 0.05, n = 7) greater than those evoked by pHo 6.2 alone (310 +/- 31 nM; Fig. 11a). Since the amplitude of the transient [Ca2+]i response to acidosis tended to vary widely between cells, these data were normalised to the Ca2+ transient evoked under normoxic conditions (set as 100%) and are presented in Fig. 11e. In addition to increasing Ca2+-transient amplitude, anoxia also appeared to slow the recovery (rate of decline) of the [Ca2+]i transient. Under control conditions, recovery rates (measured over the range 200-250 nM) were 3.7 +/- 0.5 nM/s at pHo 6.8 and 4.3 +/- 0.8 nM/s at pHo 6.2. In anoxia, these rates were slowed to 2.2 +/- 0.5 nM/s (p < 0.05, n = 8) at pHo 6.8 and 1.7 +/- 0.6 nM/s (n = 8, p < 0.01) at pHo 6.2. Ca2+ transients generated in response to anoxic-acidosis were either completely prevented or were significantly reduced in Ca2+-free medium (pHo 6.8, ∆[Ca2+]i = 11 +/- 4 nM, n = 6, p < 0.05; pHo 6.2, ∆[Ca2+]i = 79 +/- 14 nM, n = 5, p < 0.05; Fig. 11d, f). Fig. 11Effects of anoxia on acid and capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ signalling. a, b Effects of anoxia on acid (pHo 6.8 and pHo 6.2) evoked Ca2+ responses. c Effects of anoxia on Ca2+ response to capsaicin (100 nM for 10 s). d Effects of removal of extracellular Ca2+ on Ca2+ response to acidic anoxia. e Summary of the effects of anoxia on the initial Ca2+ transient generated in response to acidosis and capsaicin. Data are normalised to the control response (in absence of anoxia) which is arbitrarily set to 100%. Values are means + SEM. f Summary of the effects of Ca2+-free media on the initial [Ca2+]i transient generated in response to acidic anoxia. Data are means + SEM. Statistical significance (in e and f) was assessed using Student's paired t test (**p < 0.01; *p < 0.05). Numbers of experiments are given in parenthesis. Exposure periods (in a-d) are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars, 200 s
###end p 65
###begin p 66
###xml 49 51 49 51 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>

###xml 209 210 209 210 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d</bold>

###xml 287 288 287 288 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e</bold>
###xml 339 341 339 341 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 526 527 526 527 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">f</bold>
###xml 556 558 556 558 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>

###end p 66
###begin p 67
###xml 70 72 70 72 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 142 144 142 144 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 226 227 224 225 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 236 237 234 235 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 248 254 246 252 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig11" ref-type="fig">11c, e</xref>
###xml 297 299 295 297 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 418 419 412 413 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 425 426 419 420 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
For comparison, we also investigated the effects of anoxia upon the Ca2+ transients evoked by capsaicin (100 nM for 10 s). Capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients were also amplified under anoxic conditions to 140 +/- 6% of control (p < 0.01, n = 6; Fig. 11c, e). Moreover, the recovery rates of these Ca2+ transients were significantly slowed from 9 +/- 1.6 nM/s under normoxia to 3.4 +/- 0.8 nM/s during anoxic conditions (n = 6, p < 0.01).
###end p 67
###begin p 68
###xml 25 27 25 27 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 28 29 28 29 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 28 29 28 29 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 133 134 133 134 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 175 177 175 177 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 178 179 178 179 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 178 179 178 179 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 197 198 195 196 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 222 223 218 219 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 235 236 231 232 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 248 249 244 245 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 274 275 270 271 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 316 318 312 314 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 319 320 315 316 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 319 320 315 316 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 340 341 334 335 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 366 367 358 359 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 379 380 371 372 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 392 393 384 385 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
The sustained rise in [Ca2+]i induced by anoxia and acidosis was also significantly greater than that evoked by acidosis alone. At pHo 6.8 in anoxia, the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i was 84 +/- 9 nM (n = 13) vs. 52 +/- 6 nM (n = 17) in pHo 6.8 alone (p < 0.01). Similarly at pHo 6.2 in anoxia, the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i was 104 +/- 25 nM (n = 12) vs. 73 +/- 13 nM (n = 13) in pHo 6.2 alone (p < 0.05).
###end p 68
###begin title 69

In addition to anoxia, under ischaemic conditions, there may also be consumption of exogenous glucose. So, we also investigated the effects of combining acidosis and anoxia with aglycaemia. In the following experiments combining extracellular acidosis (pHo 6.8 or 6.2) with anoxia and aglycaemia (aaa 6.8 and aaa 6.2) resulted in a rapid transient increase in [Ca2+]i followed by a sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in the majority (65%) of neurones (see, e.g. Fig. 12a, e). ###begin p 72
###xml 121 123 121 123 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 124 125 124 125 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 124 125 124 125 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 136 148 136 148 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig12" ref-type="fig">12a, b, e, f</xref>
###xml 176 178 176 178 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 179 180 179 180 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 179 180 179 180 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 329 335 329 335 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig12" ref-type="fig">12g, h</xref>
###xml 371 372 371 372 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 383 384 383 384 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 525 527 525 527 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 528 529 528 529 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 528 529 528 529 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 549 559 549 559 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Sec20" ref-type="sec">Discussion</xref>
By comparison, the responses to anoxia alone or aglycaemia alone consisted only of a monophasic sustained increase in [Ca2+]i (see Fig. 12a, b, e, f). The sustained rise in [Ca2+]i evoked by the combination of anoxia, acidosis and aglycaemia was found to be larger than that evoked by any of these stimuli in isolation (see Fig. 12g, h; although the difference between pHo 6.8 and pHo 6.8 + anoxia and aglycaemia just failed to reach statistical significance). This suggests that the effects of these stimuli on sustained [Ca2+]i are additive (see "Discussion" section).
###end p 72
###begin p 73
###xml 18 20 18 20 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 152 154 152 154 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 155 156 155 156 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 155 156 155 156 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 351 353 351 353 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig13" ref-type="fig">13</xref>
###xml 399 401 399 401 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 429 430 429 430 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 454 455 452 453 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>

###xml 557 558 553 554 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 569 570 565 566 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 582 583 578 579 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 643 645 639 641 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 676 677 672 673 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 694 695 690 691 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 701 708 697 704 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;13</label>
###xml 724 726 720 722 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 871 872 867 868 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 883 884 879 880 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 915 916 911 912 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 920 931 916 927 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">parenthesis</italic>
###xml 971 975 967 971 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">bars</italic>
###xml 1011 1012 1007 1008 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">U</italic>
###xml 1020 1021 1016 1017 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 1032 1033 1028 1029 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 708 1041 704 1037 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="74">Comparison of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transient amplitude in response to acidosis, anoxic acidosis and anoxic aglycaemic acidosis. ###xml 708 1041 704 1037 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="74">Comparison of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transient amplitude in response to acidosis, anoxic acidosis and anoxic aglycaemic acidosis. Statistical comparisons, indicated by <italic>bars</italic>, were conducted using Mann&#8211;Whitney <italic>U</italic> test (*<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.05; **<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01)</p></caption>
###xml 1041 1041 1037 1037 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig13_HTML" id="MO13"/>
###xml 701 1041 697 1037 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig13"><label>Fig.&#160;13</label><caption><p textid="74">Comparison of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transient amplitude in response to acidosis, anoxic acidosis and anoxic aglycaemic acidosis. Studies were conducted at two levels of acidosis pH<sub>o</sub> 6.8 and pH<sub>o</sub> 6.2. Data are mean + SEM with <italic>n</italic> in <italic>parenthesis</italic>. Statistical comparisons, indicated by <italic>bars</italic>, were conducted using Mann&#8211;Whitney <italic>U</italic> test (*<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.05; **<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.01)</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig13_HTML" id="MO13"/></fig>
As noted above, Ca2+ transients were only observed under acid conditions and not with anoxia or aglycaemia alone. The amplitude of these acid-evoked [Ca2+]i transients were, however, augmented by both anoxia and the combination of anoxia and aglycaemia with the effects of anoxia and aglycaemia being larger than the effects of anoxia alone (see Fig. 13). We also noted that the recovery rates of Ca2+ transients evoked by aaa pHo 6.8 (1.9 +/- 0.5 nM/s, n = 26) and aaa pHo 6.2 (2.5 +/- 0.4 nM/s, n = 16) were again significantly reduced when compared to pHo 6.8 and pHo 6.2 alone (p < 0.05), but were not different to the recovery rates of Ca2+ transients evoked by anoxia pHo 6.2 or anoxia pHo 6.8. Fig. 13Comparison of Ca2+-transient amplitude in response to acidosis, anoxic acidosis and anoxic aglycaemic acidosis. Studies were conducted at two levels of acidosis pHo 6.8 and pHo 6.2. Data are mean + SEM with n in parenthesis. Statistical comparisons, indicated by bars, were conducted using Mann-Whitney U test (*p < 0.05; **p < 0.01)
###end p 73

###xml 4295 4295 4274 4274 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig16_HTML" id="MO16"/>
###xml 3264 4295 3245 4274 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig16"><label>Fig.&#160;16</label><caption><p textid="79">Effects of the TRPV1 inhibitors BCTC (1&#160;&#956;M) or AMG 9810 (1&#160;&#956;M, AMG) on Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients evoked by acidosis, anoxia and aglycaemia (<italic>aaa</italic>). <bold>a</bold>, <bold>b</bold> Mixed acidosis (20% CO<sub>2</sub> in pH 6.2). <bold>c</bold> Mixed acidosis with lactate (20% CO<sub>2</sub>, 15&#160;mM Na-lactate, pH 6.2). <bold>d</bold> Comparison of aaa evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transient amplitudes in the presence of BCTC and AMG 9810. Despite this wash period Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients did not fully recover following removal of either BCTC or AMG 9810. Data are means + SEM; statistical significance was assessed using Student&#8217;s paired <italic>t</italic> test (***<italic>p</italic>&#8201;&lt;&#8201;0.001). Exposure periods are indicated by <italic>horizontal bars</italic>. <italic>Time scale bars</italic> are 200&#160;s</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="424_2009_715_Fig16_HTML" id="MO16"/></fig>
As previously observed for acidosis alone, the initial Ca2+ transient in response to aaa was greatly inhibited in Ca2+-free media whereas the sustained elevation in [Ca2+]i was independent of external Ca2+ (see Fig. 14). Having determined that the initial Ca2+ transients in response to the combination of acidosis, anoxia and aglycaemia is dependent upon Ca2+ influx, we next sought to identify the ion channels responsible for this Ca2+ influx. Neurones were exposed to aaa pHo 6.8 alone (control) and then to aaa 6.8 in the presence of amiloride, Ni2+, capsazepine, Gd3+ and then to aaa 6.8 again (second control). The amplitudes of the resulting Ca2+ transients were normalised to the first control response (arbitrarily set as 100%). Amiloride (100 muM) reduced Ca2+ transients to 50 +/- 8% (n = 6, p < 0.01) compared to control (Fig. 15a, e). Ni2+ (2.5 mM) had no significant effect on Ca2+ transients (81 +/- 21%, n = 5, p = 0.413; Fig. 15b, e). Capsazepine (10 muM) also had no significant effect upon the Ca2+ transient (86 +/- 7% compared to control, n = 5, p = 0.1; Fig. 15c, e). In the presence of Gd3+ (1 mM), Ca2+ transients evoked by aaa pHo 6.8 were reduced to 45 +/- 9% (n = 6, p < 0.05; Fig. 15d, e). BCTC (1 muM) and AMG 9810 also inhibited Ca2+ transients evoked by aaa pHo 6.2 (Fig. 16a, b, d). Ca2+ transients were reduced to 9 +/- 6% (n = 6, p < 0.001) by BCTC and to 11 +/- 8% (n = 6, p < 0.001) by AMG 9810, respectively. Ca2+ transients evoked by lactic acidosis in the presence of anoxia and aglycaemia (20% CO2 in 15 mM Na-lactate) were similarly reduced by AMG 9810 to 11 +/- 5% (n = 6, p < 0.001; Fig. 16c, d). The effects of BCTC and AMG 9810 were again largely irreversible within the time frame of these experiments (Fig. 16a-c). Thus, the Ca2+ transient evoked by aaa was strongly inhibited by BCTC and AMG 9810 (although not completely abolished) was roughly halved by amiloride and Gd3+ but was unaffected by Ni2+ or capsazepine. Fig. 14a, b Effects of removal of extracellular Ca2+ upon [Ca2+]i responses to combined acidosis (a pHo 6.8, b pHo 6.2) anoxia and aglycaemia. The initial Ca2+ transient in b was out of scale. c, d Summary of the effects of Ca2+ removal on both the [Ca2+]i transient and the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in response to combined acidosis anoxia and aglycaemia. Statistical significance was assessed by Student's paired t test (*p < 0.05; **p < 0.01). Numbers of experiments are given in parentheses. Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars, 100 sFig. 15Identification of calcium entry pathways activated in acidic (pHo 6.8) anoxic aglycaemia. a Effects of the ASIC inhibitor amiloride (100 muM). b Effects of the voltage-gated Ca2+-channel inhibitor Ni2+ (2.5 mM). c Effects of the capsaicin antagonist capsazepine (CPZ; 10 muM). d Effects of the trivalent cation Gd3+. e Summary of effects of amiloride, Ni2+ capsazepine and Gd3+ on Ca2+-transient amplitude in response to acidic anoxic aglycaemia. Data are normalised to the control transient recorded in the absence of drug (set to 100%). Statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (*p < 0.05; **p < 0.01; ***p < 0.001). Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars are 200 sFig. 16Effects of the TRPV1 inhibitors BCTC (1 muM) or AMG 9810 (1 muM, AMG) on Ca2+ transients evoked by acidosis, anoxia and aglycaemia (aaa). a, b Mixed acidosis (20% CO2 in pH 6.2). c Mixed acidosis with lactate (20% CO2, 15 mM Na-lactate, pH 6.2). d Comparison of aaa evoked Ca2+-transient amplitudes in the presence of BCTC and AMG 9810. Ca2+-transient amplitude is expressed as a percentage of the first control response to aaa. Note that both inhibitors reduced initial Ca2+ transients significantly (for both types of acidosis) but did not completely abolish them. Interruptions in the recordings between the application of the inhibitor and the second control were approximately 10 min (to allow for washout of AMG 9810 or BCTC). Despite this wash period Ca2+ transients did not fully recover following removal of either BCTC or AMG 9810. Data are means + SEM; statistical significance was assessed using Student's paired t test (***p < 0.001). Exposure periods are indicated by horizontal bars. Time scale bars are 200 s
###end p 76
