###begin title 80
Discussion
###end title 80
###begin p 81
###xml 89 91 89 91 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 92 93 92 93 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 92 93 92 93 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 228 230 228 230 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR68">68</xref>
###xml 302 304 302 304 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">25</xref>
###xml 306 308 306 308 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR73">73</xref>
###xml 310 313 310 313 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR113">113</xref>
###xml 422 424 422 424 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 437 439 437 439 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR23">23</xref>
###xml 441 443 441 443 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR28">28</xref>
###xml 445 447 445 447 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR56">56</xref>
In this study, we have investigated the effects of acidosis, anoxia and aglycaemia on [Ca2+]i in sensory neurones. Acidosis is considered to be one of the primary stimuli responsible for sensory neurone excitation in ischaemia [68]. It also plays a pivotal role in triggering neuronal release of CGRP [25, 73, 113]. The secretion CGRP in response to a number of stimuli, including acidosis, is critically dependent upon Ca2+ signalling [23, 28, 56].
###end p 81
###begin p 82
###xml 99 101 99 101 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 102 103 102 103 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 102 103 102 103 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 248 250 248 250 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">17</xref>
###xml 252 254 252 254 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR68">68</xref>
###xml 256 258 256 258 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR88">88</xref>
###xml 453 455 453 455 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 488 490 488 490 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 492 494 492 494 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR55">55</xref>
###xml 496 498 496 498 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR69">69</xref>
###xml 500 502 500 502 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR75">75</xref>
In the first part of this study, we investigated the effects of acidosis alone on intracellular [Ca2+]i in small capsaicin-sensitive neurones arising from dorsal root ganglia as these are thought to be responsible for sensing ischaemic conditions [17, 68, 88]. In the second part of the study, we have compounded the effects of acidosis with anoxia and aglycaemia since these conditions not only accompany ischaemia but can have profound effects upon Ca2+ regulation in sensory neurones [34, 55, 69, 75].
###end p 82
###begin title 83
###xml 32 34 32 34 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 35 36 35 36 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 35 36 35 36 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
Effects of acidosis alone on [Ca2+]i in sensory neurones
###end title 83
###begin p 84
###xml 130 131 130 131 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 262 263 262 263 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 428 429 428 429 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">o</sub>
###xml 534 536 534 536 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 538 540 538 540 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR52">52</xref>
###xml 542 545 542 545 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR110">110</xref>
###xml 604 606 604 606 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 607 608 607 608 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 607 608 607 608 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 694 696 694 696 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 825 827 825 827 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 835 837 835 837 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 897 899 897 899 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 900 901 900 901 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 900 901 900 901 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 941 943 941 943 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 944 945 944 945 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 944 945 944 945 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 979 981 979 981 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1044 1046 1044 1046 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
We have employed four different levels and types of acidosis in this study: a relatively mild hypercapnic acidosis (pH 6.8; 20% CO2 and normal bicarbonate) representative of the early stages of ischaemia in the heart, a more severe mixed acidosis (pH 6.2; 20% CO2 and reduced bicarbonate) and an equivalent mixed acidosis including 15 mM Na-lactate representative of the latter stages of ischaemia and a very severe acidosis (pHo 5.0) representative of the stimuli frequently used to activate acid sensitive cation channels in vitro [10, 52, 110]. All four acid stimuli evoked a biphasic elevation of [Ca2+]i in the majority of neurones investigated. The initial phase was characterised by a Ca2+ transient occurring within the first minute of exposure to acidosis. This initial transient was chiefly dependent on external Ca2+. The Ca2+ transient was then followed by a sustained elevation of [Ca2+]i. This second sustained phase of the [Ca2+]i response was not depended upon Ca2+ influx. We will first address possible mechanisms for these Ca2+ signalling events.
###end p 84
###begin title 85
###xml 36 38 36 38 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Pharmacological identification of Ca2+ influx pathways
###end title 85
###begin p 86
###xml 45 47 45 47 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 100 103 100 103 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR101">101</xref>
###xml 105 108 105 108 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR102">102</xref>
###xml 110 113 110 113 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR110">110</xref>
###xml 152 154 152 154 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">17</xref>
###xml 156 158 156 158 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR89">89</xref>
###xml 184 186 184 186 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 246 248 246 248 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">15</xref>
###xml 250 252 250 252 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">40</xref>
###xml 254 256 254 256 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR87">87</xref>
The most likely mechanisms for acid-evoked Ca2+ influx in neurones are through (a) ASIC1a channels [101, 102, 110], (b) TRPV1 (or capsaicin) receptors [17, 89] and (c) voltage-gated Ca2+ channels secondary to acid-evoked membrane depolarisation [15, 40, 87]. In order to discriminate between these entry pathways, we needed to find suitable pharmacological tools.
###end p 86
###begin p 87
###xml 64 67 64 67 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR106">106</xref>
###xml 162 164 162 164 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 175 177 175 177 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR86">86</xref>
###xml 179 181 179 181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR95">95</xref>
###xml 321 324 321 324 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR108">108</xref>
ASIC channels are widely reported to be inhibited by amiloride [106] although this is not entirely selective as amiloride may also inhibit T-type voltage-gated Ca2+ channels [86, 95]. Recent evidence also indicates that under moderate acidosis (pH 7.0), amiloride may activate ASIC3 leading to sustained inward currents [108].
###end p 87
###begin p 88
###xml 62 64 62 64 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 95 96 95 96 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR3">3</xref>
###xml 98 99 98 99 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR4">4</xref>
###xml 274 275 274 275 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">9</xref>
###xml 277 279 277 279 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">13</xref>
###xml 281 283 281 283 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR62">62</xref>
###xml 285 287 285 287 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR64">64</xref>
###xml 289 291 289 291 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR71">71</xref>
###xml 293 295 293 295 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR74">74</xref>
###xml 297 299 297 299 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR95">95</xref>
###xml 376 378 375 377 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 414 416 413 415 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 447 449 446 448 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 523 525 522 524 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 612 614 611 613 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 642 644 641 643 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 687 689 686 688 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 695 697 694 696 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 818 820 817 819 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 951 953 950 952 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1002 1004 1001 1003 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1018 1020 1017 1019 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1093 1095 1092 1094 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1149 1151 1148 1150 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1163 1165 1162 1164 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1167 1169 1166 1168 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">36</xref>
###xml 1174 1176 1173 1175 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1342 1344 1341 1343 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1363 1365 1362 1364 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1423 1425 1422 1424 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1480 1482 1479 1481 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1524 1526 1523 1525 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1528 1530 1527 1529 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR66">66</xref>
###xml 1575 1577 1574 1576 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1587 1589 1586 1588 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1644 1646 1643 1645 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1748 1750 1747 1749 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1881 1882 1880 1881 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 1889 1891 1888 1890 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1924 1925 1923 1924 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR1">1</xref>
###xml 1973 1975 1972 1974 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1982 1984 1981 1983 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2057 2059 2056 2058 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Although there are several different types of voltage-gated Ca2+ channels in sensory neurones [3, 4], there are a number of divalent cations that can be used to inhibit all high voltage-activated channels (cadmium) and both high and low voltage-activated channels (nickel) [9, 13, 62, 64, 71, 74, 95]. Using high potassium solutions to depolarise, we confirmed that 100 muM Cd2+ substantially blocked and 2.5 mM Ni2+ fully blocked voltage-gated Ca2+ entry. We next tested the effects of these cations on capsaicin evoked Ca2+ influx to see if they could be used to discriminate between TRPV1 and voltage-gated Ca2+ entry. Capsaicin-induced Ca2+ transients were not inhibited by either Ni2+ or Cd2+; indeed, the effects of capsaicin appeared to be potentiated. In particular, exposure to capsaicin in the presence of Cd2+ led to a substantial and mostly irreversible increase in the Fura-2 fluorescence ratio. We suggest that this is probably due to Cd2+ entry through TRPV1 and subsequent binding of Cd2+ to Fura-2. Cd2+ binding to Fura-2 has similar effects on its fluorescence to those of Ca2+ binding but Fura-2 has a much greater affinity for Cd2+ than for Ca2+ [36]. Ni2+ ions can also bind to Fura-2 but when they do so, they quench fluorescence. No fluorescence quenching was observed during exposure to capsaicin in the presence of Ni2+ suggesting that Ni2+ does not pass through TRPV1 to any significant extent. Ni2+ was also ineffective in inhibiting capsaicin-evoked Mn2+ entry (TRPV1 channels are permeable to Mn2+ [66]), whereas it fully blocked voltage-gated Mn2+ entry. Mn2+ quenching of Fura-2 fluorescence in the presence of Ni2+ may therefore also serve as an indicator of TRPV1 activation. One possible problem with the use of Ni2+ is that it appeared to slightly potentiate the effects of capsaicin. Sensitisation of TRPV1 channels by other external cations, Na+ and Mg2+, has been previously described [1]. Despite this sensitisation, the effects of Ni2+ and Cd2+ should permit discrimination between voltage-gated and TRPV1-mediated Ca2+ influx.
###end p 88
###begin p 89
###xml 106 108 106 108 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR31">31</xref>
###xml 110 112 110 112 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR51">51</xref>
###xml 114 116 114 116 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR53">53</xref>
###xml 253 255 253 255 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 291 293 291 293 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 396 397 396 397 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">7</xref>
###xml 399 401 399 401 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR14">14</xref>
###xml 403 405 403 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR49">49</xref>
###xml 407 409 407 409 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR62">62</xref>
###xml 414 416 414 416 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 588 589 587 588 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR1">1</xref>
###xml 591 593 590 592 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR92">92</xref>
###xml 620 622 619 621 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 658 660 657 659 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 690 692 689 691 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 746 748 745 747 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 767 769 766 768 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 929 931 927 929 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">27</xref>
###xml 933 935 931 933 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR93">93</xref>
###xml 992 994 990 992 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
The TRPV1 antagonist capsazepine was, as expected, highly efficient in blocking the effects of capsaicin [31, 51, 53]. Whether it can also block acid-evoked activation of TRPV1 is, however, uncertain (see below). We therefore sought other blockers of Ca2+ entry through TRPV1. Gadolinium (Gd3+) is a small lanthanide that blocks various types of calcium channels at submillimolar concentrations [7, 14, 49, 62]. Gd3+ has been reported to activate and sensitise TRPV1 channels at low concentrations, but at higher concentrations (>300 muM), it inhibits capsaicin-evoked membrane currents [1, 92]. In the present study, Gd3+ inhibited both voltage-activated Ca2+ entry and capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ influx, but we could only inhibit capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients with Gd3+ concentrations of 500 muM and above. Two newer TRPV1 antagonists BCTC and AMG 9810 with reported ability to block proton activation of TRPV1 were also tested [27, 93]. These produced a profound block of capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients that was largely irreversible.
###end p 89
###begin title 90
###xml 14 16 14 16 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Acid-evoked Ca2+ influx pathways
###end title 90
###begin p 91
###xml 115 117 115 117 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 200 202 200 202 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">29</xref>
###xml 204 206 204 206 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR57">57</xref>
###xml 208 210 208 210 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR67">67</xref>
###xml 212 214 212 214 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR77">77</xref>
###xml 377 379 377 379 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 418 420 418 420 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 436 438 436 438 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">41</xref>
###xml 440 442 440 442 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">42</xref>
###xml 444 446 444 446 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR78">78</xref>
###xml 510 511 510 511 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 543 545 543 545 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 547 549 547 549 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR19">19</xref>
###xml 551 553 551 553 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR99">99</xref>
###xml 555 558 555 558 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR105">105</xref>
###xml 560 563 560 563 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR110">110</xref>
###xml 590 592 590 592 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 634 637 634 637 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR107">107</xref>
###xml 748 750 748 750 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 763 765 763 765 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 767 769 767 769 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR18">18</xref>
###xml 771 773 771 773 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR99">99</xref>
###xml 869 871 869 871 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 971 973 971 973 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1112 1114 1112 1114 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Protons activate strong inward sodium currents in DRG neurones which have both transient and sustained components [10]. These currents are mediated mainly by ASIC3, a proton-sensitive cation channel [29, 57, 67, 77]. ASICs are thought to play a key role in transmitting ischaemic pain. They are directly activated by extracellular acidosis which relieves channel blockade by Ca2+ ions through the protonation of the Ca2+-binding sides [41, 42, 78]. The ion selectivity of these channels is not restricted to Na+; ASIC1a is also permeable to Ca2+ [19, 99, 105, 110] and contributes to the Ca2+ overload in neurones of ischaemic brain [107]. In the present study, amiloride was administered to evaluate the contribution of ASICs to the acid-evoked Ca2+ transients [10, 18, 99]. Surprisingly although there was a general tendency for amiloride to reduce the acid-evoked Ca2+ transient at all pH values tested, this effect did not reach statistical significance and robust Ca2+ transients invariably remained. Thus, although we cannot completely exclude a minor role for ASICs in mediating some of the acid-evoked Ca2+ influx, they are clearly not the main cause.
###end p 91
###begin p 92
###xml 32 34 32 34 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 41 43 41 43 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 53 55 53 55 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 135 137 135 137 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 151 153 151 153 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 191 193 191 193 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 211 213 211 213 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4c</xref>
###xml 247 249 247 249 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 346 348 346 348 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 508 510 508 510 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 561 563 561 563 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 579 581 579 581 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 640 642 639 641 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 740 742 739 741 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 762 764 761 763 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 771 773 770 772 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 801 803 800 802 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 983 985 982 984 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1007 1008 1006 1007 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
We also tested the effects of Ni2+ and Cd2+ on the Ca2+ response to acidosis. Both of these agents failed to inhibit the acid-evoked Ca2+ transient. Ni2+ also failed to inhibit acid-evoked Mn2+ influx (see Fig. 4c). These data indicate that the Ca2+ transient is not primarily a consequence of membrane depolarisation followed by voltage-gated Ca2+ entry and point instead to another acid-activated cation channel. The fact that acid evoked a sustained rise in Fura-2 fluorescence ratio in the presence of Cd2+ suggests that this channel is also permeable to Cd2+. Acid-evoked Ca2+ transients were, however, completely blocked by 500 muM Gd3+ and blocked by 85-90% by BCTC and AMG 9810. These characteristics, resistance/sensitisation by Ni2+, permeability to Mn2+ and Cd2+, block by high levels of Gd3+ and block by BCTC and AMG 9810 are identical to those of TRPV1 as described above. We also noted a positive correlation between the amplitudes of acidosis- and capsaicin-evoked Ca2+ transients (see Fig. 2).
###end p 92
###begin p 93
###xml 71 73 71 73 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 149 151 149 151 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 507 509 506 508 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR89">89</xref>
###xml 589 591 587 589 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR61">61</xref>
###xml 593 595 591 593 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR65">65</xref>
###xml 731 733 729 731 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR52">52</xref>
###xml 823 825 821 823 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">11</xref>
###xml 827 829 825 827 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR98">98</xref>
###xml 1054 1056 1052 1054 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR45">45</xref>
###xml 1107 1109 1105 1107 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR43">43</xref>
###xml 1491 1493 1489 1491 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 294 297 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 330 333 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 466 469 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 611 614 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 1218 1221 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 1345 1348 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
Although the above data point to TRPV1 as being the main pathway for Ca2+ entry, it was notable that capsazepine failed to inhibit the acid-evoked Ca2+ transients. There are conflicting reports in the literature regarding the capsazepine sensitivity of both proton-activated inward currents in rat sensory neurones and the cloned rat TRPV1 channel. Tominaga and Tominaga originally reported that 10 muM capsazepine inhibited proton activated currents through cloned rat TRPV1 channels by approximately 80% [89], but others have subsequently found no effect at concentrations up to 30 muM [61, 65]. Similarly in rat sensory neurones, there are some reports that the sustained proton-activated currents are inhibited by capsazepine [52] and others which show no effect of capsazepine on proton-evoked currents or ion fluxes [11, 98]. Absence of effects of capsazepine upon proton activation of TRPV1 is not surprising since the capsaicin/capsazepine binding site and proton binding site are at different locations (capsaicin binds an intracellular domain [45] whereas protons bind to an extracellular domain [43]). The lack of consistency with respect to the effects of capsazepine on proton-activated TRPV1 currents in rat is, however, puzzling. Nevertheless, as there is clear precedent for lack of effect of capsazepine on proton activation of rat TRPV1 and given the strong inhibitory effects of two other TRPV1 antagonists, the failure of capsazepine to antagonise the proton-activated Ca2+ influx cannot be considered to exclude a role for TRPV1.
###end p 93
###begin p 94
###xml 48 50 48 50 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 151 153 151 153 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 242 244 242 244 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 301 303 301 303 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 465 467 465 467 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">10</xref>
###xml 469 471 469 471 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">42</xref>
###xml 473 475 473 475 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR78">78</xref>
###xml 486 488 486 488 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 565 567 565 567 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
In summary, the primary route for acid-evoked Ca2+ influx is via TRPV1. We cannot entirely exclude some contributory role for parallel voltage-gated Ca2+ entry (secondary to membrane depolarisation) since possible sensitisation of TRPV1 by Ni2+ may obscure any minor contribution from voltage-gated Ca2+ channels. The lack of any apparent major role for ASIC channels may seem surprising, but it should be noted that many of these channels inactivate very rapidly [10, 42, 78] so any Ca2+ influx will be short-lived and could easily be obscured by the subsequent Ca2+ influx through TRPV1.
###end p 94
###begin title 95
###xml 2 4 2 4 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Ca2+ liberation from internal stores during external acidosis
###end title 95
###begin p 96
###xml 56 58 56 58 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 59 60 59 60 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 59 60 59 60 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 88 89 88 89 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">o</sub>
###xml 89 91 89 91 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" arrange="stack">2+</sup>
###xml 125 127 125 127 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 128 129 128 129 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 128 129 128 129 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 182 184 182 184 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 244 246 244 246 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 299 301 299 301 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 328 329 328 329 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</sup>
###xml 394 396 394 396 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 397 398 397 398 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 397 398 397 398 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 408 410 408 410 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 511 513 511 513 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 514 515 514 515 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 514 515 514 515 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 562 564 562 564 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 647 649 647 649 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 650 651 650 651 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 650 651 650 651 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 752 754 752 754 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
During exposure to acidosis, there is also a rise in [Ca2+]i that is independent from Cao2+. The sustained nature of this [Ca2+]i rise suggests a change in the equilibrium between Ca2+ fluxes into and out of the cytosol (note that a discrete Ca2+-release event alone, for example, displacement of Ca2+ from internal buffers by H+, would be expected to only result in a transient increase in [Ca2+]i as the Ca2+ released is subsequently extruded). We have not investigated the cause of this sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in any detail, but we have observed that ER Ca2+-store depletion using either caffeine or CPA reduces this acid induced rise in [Ca2+]i. One possible explanation therefore is that intracellular acidosis causes a sustained increase in Ca2+ leak from the ER.
###end p 96
###begin p 97
###xml 121 122 121 122 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">6</xref>
###xml 124 126 124 126 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">84</xref>
###xml 173 175 173 175 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 287 289 287 289 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR39">39</xref>
###xml 291 293 291 293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR54">54</xref>
###xml 295 297 295 297 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR90">90</xref>
###xml 442 444 442 444 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR38">38</xref>
###xml 492 494 492 494 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 531 533 531 533 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 654 656 654 656 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 657 658 657 658 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 657 658 657 658 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 700 702 700 702 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 766 768 766 768 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 874 876 874 876 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Whilst a number of studies indicate that a direct effect of intracellular acidosis is to inhibit the ryanodine receptor [6, 84], there is another indirect pathway whereby Ca2+ release from internal stores may be triggered by the activation of proton-sensing G protein-coupled receptors [39, 54, 90]. These receptors have recently been described in approximately 75% of small nociceptive DRG neurones in which they are colocalised with ASICs [38]. In addition to the possibility of enhanced Ca2+ release, acidosis may also reduce Ca2+ reuptake into the ER by inhibiting SERCA. Moreover, our observation that a small (acid evoked) sustained increase in [Ca2+]i also occurs in the presence of CPA (in Ca2+-free media) suggests that some inhibition of plasma membrane Ca2+ extrusion is also likely. Determining which of these pathways are indeed responsible for the sustained Ca2+ release from internal stores seen acidic conditions clearly requires further investigation.
###end p 97
###begin title 98
Effects of combining acidic stimuli with anoxia and aglycaemia
###end title 98
###begin p 99
###xml 134 136 134 136 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 153 155 153 155 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig13" ref-type="fig">13</xref>
###xml 401 403 401 403 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 524 526 524 526 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 617 619 617 619 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 640 642 640 642 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 667 669 667 669 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 784 786 784 786 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1153 1155 1153 1155 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR26">26</xref>
###xml 1180 1182 1180 1182 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1307 1309 1307 1309 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1386 1388 1386 1388 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1442 1444 1442 1444 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1478 1480 1478 1480 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1489 1491 1489 1491 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 1550 1552 1550 1552 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1657 1659 1657 1659 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 1661 1663 1661 1663 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">35</xref>
###xml 1730 1732 1730 1732 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1878 1880 1878 1880 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1881 1882 1881 1882 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1881 1882 1881 1882 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1922 1924 1922 1924 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1925 1926 1925 1926 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1925 1926 1925 1926 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1970 1973 1970 1973 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig11" ref-type="fig">11c</xref>
###xml 2023 2025 2023 2025 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2052 2053 2052 2053 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2</xref>
###xml 2055 2057 2055 2057 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR63">63</xref>
###xml 2059 2061 2059 2061 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR79">79</xref>
###xml 2063 2065 2063 2065 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR83">83</xref>
###xml 2067 2070 2067 2070 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR103">103</xref>
###xml 2128 2130 2128 2130 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2174 2176 2174 2176 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2392 2394 2392 2394 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2396 2398 2396 2398 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 2400 2402 2400 2402 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">35</xref>
###xml 2468 2470 2468 2470 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2567 2569 2567 2569 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 2583 2585 2583 2585 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1125 1130 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
When acidosis was combined with anoxia, or with both anoxia and aglycaemia, there was a systematic increase in the amplitude of the Ca2+ transient (Fig. 13). The effects of acidosis are therefore potentiated by loss of oxygen and loss of glucose both of which are conditions present in ischaemia. There are likely to be a number of factors contributing to this effect. In analysing the cause of the Ca2+ transient in anoxia, aglycaemia and acidosis, we found that this transient was exclusively dependent on extracellular Ca2+ and was strongly inhibited by BCTC and AMG 9810, but was not inhibited by voltage-gated Ca2+-channel blockers (Ni2+). The major source of Ca2+ influx would therefore again seem to be mainly via TRPV1. We did, however, note that amiloride also reduced the Ca2+ transient under these conditions. A role for ASICs would therefore seem to be more prominent in response to anoxic-aglycaemic-acidosis than to acidosis alone. One possible explanation for this is that oxygen and glucose deprivation has recently been reported to both increase the amplitude and slow the inactivation of ASIC1a currents in mouse hippocampal neurones [26]. Thus, the amount of Ca2+ influx through ASIC1a may be enhanced. Further factors which we anticipate will also contribute to the enhancement of the Ca2+ transient by anoxia and aglycaemia include rather more general changes to Ca2+ metabolism including inhibition of plasma membrane Ca2+ extrusion and inhibition of SR Ca2+ uptake [34]. Moderate inhibition of both SERCA and plasma membrane Ca2+ ATPase (PMCA) pumps occurs fairly rapidly in anoxia and corresponds to decline in cytosolic ATP levels [34, 35]. These factors probably account for the slower recovery of the Ca2+ transient observed in anoxic-aglycaemic-acidosis compared to that seen in acidosis alone. Indeed, we also noted that anoxia both enhanced the [Ca2+]i response to capsaicin and prolonged [Ca2+]i recovery following capsaicin removal (Fig. 11c). Given the prominent role for mitochondria in Ca2+ buffering in these cells [2, 63, 79, 83, 103], one might also anticipate that reduced mitochondrial Ca2+ uptake should contribute to the enhanced Ca2+ transient. In previous studies, however, we have found that although relatively brief anoxia does cause partial mitochondrial depolarisation, this does not appear to affect the capacity of mitochondria to take up Ca2+ [34, 35]. In summary, the causes of the enhancement of the acid evoked Ca2+ transient in anoxia and aglycaemia are likely to be complex, involving the modulation of both Ca2+-influx and Ca2+-efflux pathways and are in need of further investigation.
###end p 99
###begin p 100
###xml 72 74 72 74 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 149 150 149 150 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 150 153 150 153 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">1/2</sub>
###xml 167 169 167 169 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig11" ref-type="fig">11</xref>
###xml 309 311 309 311 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">42</xref>
###xml 313 315 313 315 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR78">78</xref>
###xml 467 469 467 469 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR91">91</xref>
###xml 471 473 471 473 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR94">94</xref>
###xml 475 478 475 478 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR112">112</xref>
###xml 506 508 506 508 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 563 565 563 565 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 566 567 566 567 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 566 567 566 567 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 602 604 602 604 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 684 686 684 686 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 687 688 687 688 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 687 688 687 688 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 839 841 839 841 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig12" ref-type="fig">12</xref>
###xml 916 918 916 918 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 977 979 977 979 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 980 981 980 981 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 980 981 980 981 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 1111 1113 1111 1113 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1135 1137 1135 1137 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1146 1148 1146 1148 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
Even in the presence of anoxia and aglycaemia, it is notable that the Ca2+-influx phase of the response to acidosis is still relatively short-lived (t1/2 = 43 s; Fig. 11). There are a number of possible explanations for this: Firstly, intrinsic inactivation leads to rapid (seconds) decline in ASIC activity [42, 78], secondly, elevated intracellular calcium leads to inactivation of TRPV1, and finally, declining ATP levels may contribute to loss of TRPV1 activity [91, 94, 112]. What remains after the Ca2+ transient has subsided is a sustained elevation of [Ca2+]i which is largely independent of Ca2+ influx. Aglycaemia and anoxia alone can also induce a sustained increase in [Ca2+]i. The response to the combination of all three 'stimuli' is greater than that observed in response to any single stimulus given in isolation (see Fig. 12). Thus, the effects of anoxia, aglycaemia and acidosis on intracellular Ca2+ regulation appear to summate. The cause of the rise in [Ca2+]i in response to anoxia has been described previously and includes inhibition of both SERCA and PMCA as well as enhanced leak of Ca2+ from internal (ER) Ca2+ stores [34]. The cause of the sustained rise in response to acidosis has not yet been investigated in full, but our preliminary data indicate that it is likely to involve changes to ER calcium handling and hint at a possible role for the recently discovered G protein-coupled proton receptors.
###end p 100
###begin title 101
Conclusions
###end title 101
###begin p 102
###xml 155 157 155 157 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 249 251 249 251 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 290 292 290 292 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">34</xref>
###xml 294 296 294 296 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">35</xref>
###xml 442 444 442 444 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 520 522 520 522 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 523 524 523 524 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 523 524 523 524 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 622 624 622 624 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
In this study, we have only investigated events likely to occur early in ischaemia; more prolonged ischaemic conditions are predicted to further disturb Ca2+ homeostasis as endogenous glycolytic fuel reserves are depleted, ATP levels collapse and Ca2+-extrusion mechanisms fail completely [34, 35]. What is interesting to note, however, is that even before this stage is reached, anoxia and aglycaemia have a marked influence over cellular Ca2+ homeostasis enhancing both the calcium influx and the sustained rise in [Ca2+]i in response to acidosis. Even under anoxic and aglycaemic conditions, however, the acid evoked Ca2+ influx, mediated predominantly via TRPV1, remains relatively short-lived.
###end p 102
###begin p 103
###xml 68 70 68 70 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 246 248 246 248 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 270 272 270 272 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 405 407 405 407 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 439 441 439 441 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 442 443 442 443 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 442 443 442 443 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>i</italic></sub>
###xml 533 535 533 535 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Important questions that remain to be addressed are how might the Ca2+-signalling events described here be further influenced by other mediators released in ischaemia (e.g. bradykinin and adenosine), could these support, enhance or prolong the Ca2+-influx phase or is Ca2+ influx only relevant in the very early stages of ischaemia? If the latter, what are the functional consequences of the sustained (Ca2+-influx independent) rise in [Ca2+]i in these cells, can it promote continued release of CGRP or is this only linked to the Ca2+-influx phase? Finding answers to these questions is important if we are to gain a better understanding of the role played by acid-sensing cation channels, calcium stores and calcium signalling in general in sensory neurone function under ischaemic conditions.
###end p 103

###begin p 224
###xml 86 88 86 88 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 300 302 300 302 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3+</sup>
###xml 470 472 470 472 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 484 486 484 486 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3d</xref>
Note that whilst under control conditions we were usually able to obtain consistent Ca2+ responses to repeated applications of capsaicin (i.e. without obvious receptor desensitisation), we cannot exclude the possibility that lack of full recovery of the capsaicin response following application of Gd3+ could be due to enhanced desensitisation rather than persistent channel block per se. We also on occasion noted failure of the capsaicin response postapplication of Ni2+ (e.g. Fig. 3d).
